The Ninth Proof: God Punishing Created Disbelief Would Be Worse Than Satan
A reductio ad absurdum argument comparing God's hypothetical action to Satan's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that if God creates disbelief and then punishes it, God would be more harmful than Satan, and how this serves as a reductio ad absurdum proof against the view that God creates evil deeds.

Core Idea
Forcing evil and punishing it is morally worse than merely inviting to it.

The author presents a powerful moral comparison. If God were to create disbelief in a person and then punish them for it, God's harm would exceed that of Satan, who only invites to evil. This comparison exposes the absurdity of the opposing view: it would make God more blameworthy than Satan, which is impossible for the All-Wise and All-Just.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow: starting with a supposition, then a comparison, then a Quranic citation, then a conclusion.
Notice the force of the word 'يلجئهم' (forces them) vs. 'دعوتكم' (I called you).
2
Identify the key comparison
Find the sentence where the author contrasts Satan's action (only inviting) with God's hypothetical action (forcing and creating). Understand why this contrast is crucial.
The author quotes Quran 14:22 to support Satan's limited role.
3
Understand the absurd conclusion
The author states that under the opposing view, it would be 'good' for the disbeliever to thank Satan and blame God. Recognize that this is considered a reductio ad absurdum: since this is clearly false, the premise (God creates disbelief and punishes it) must also be false.
The phrase 'يحسن وعقاباً' is ambiguous; the intended meaning is that it would be morally appropriate for the disbeliever to thank Satan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : لو كان تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر ، ثم يعذبه عليه لكان ضرره على العبد أشد من ضرر الشيطان [ لأنه (۱) ] لا يمكنه أن يلجئهم إلى القبح ، بل الغاية القصوى منه : أن يدعوهم إلى القبح . كما حكى الله تعالى ذلك عنه في قوله : ( وما كان لي عليكم من سلطان . إلا أن دعوتكم (۲) ) وأيضاً : فدعاء الشيطان أيضاً من فعل الله تعالى . وأما الله سبحانه ، فإنه يلجئهم إلى القبائح ويخلقها فيهم. ولو كان كذلك ، لكان يحسن وعقاباً من الكافر أن يمنح الشيطان ، وأن يذم الله سبحانه - تعالى الله علواً كبيراً
The Ninth Proof: If He, exalted, were to create disbelief in the disbeliever, then punish him for it, then His harm upon the servant would be more severe than the harm of Satan, because he (Satan) cannot force them to ugliness, but his utmost is that he calls them to ugliness, as God, exalted, related that about him in His saying: 'And I had no authority over you except that I called you' (Ibrahim 14:22). Also, the call of Satan is also an act of God, exalted. But God, glorified, He forces them to ugly deeds and creates them in them. And if that were so, then it would be good and a punishment that the disbeliever thank Satan, and blame God, glorified – exalted is God greatly.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage compares God's action (if He creates disbelief and then punishes) to Satan's action. Satan only invites to evil; he does not force it. God, on the other hand, is described (under the opposing view) as forcing disbelief and creating it in people, then punishing them for it. This would make God's harm greater than Satan's. Furthermore, if God forces disbelief, it would be appropriate for the disbeliever to thank Satan (who only invited) and blame God (who forced). The author considers this conclusion absurd and thus refutes the premise.

Key Terms
الكفر
الشيطان
الإلجاء
الدعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because God is the Creator of all things, it is just for Him to create disbelief and then punish it, as He is the owner and can do as He wills.
Correction: This proof shows that such a position would make God morally worse than Satan, which is impossible. The argument does not deny God's power, but rather uses moral reasoning to show that if God punishes what He creates, He would be unjust – which cannot be attributed to Him.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, why would God's harm be more severe than Satan's if He creates disbelief and punishes it?
A) Because God is more powerful.
B) Because Satan only invites to evil, while God would force it.
C) Because Satan is not capable of punishment.
D) Because God's punishment is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The author uses a moral comparison to show that punishing created disbelief is unjust, as it would make God more blameworthy than Satan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself resenting God for a sin you committed, as if He made you do it? How does this proof expose that resentment as a hidden inclination to thank Satan instead of God? What will you do the next time that feeling arises?
?
This argument shows that God does not force evil. Yet, do you sometimes passively accept your own sins as 'fate' or 'God's will', avoiding the responsibility to struggle against them? In light of this lesson, what is one specific sin you will stop excusing and actively fight starting today?
?
The passage ends with 'Exalted is God greatly'. Does this proof truly elevate God's justice in your heart, or is it just an intellectual exercise? If it has not changed how you trust God when you suffer, what step can you take to let this truth transform your relationship with Him?

The Tenth Proof: Without Human Agency, Reward and Punishment Are Arbitrary
A proof that human agency is necessary for just reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the tenth proof insists that humans must be the originators of their own actions for reward and punishment to be just.

Core Idea
Without human agency, divine reward and punishment become arbitrary and unjust.

The tenth proof tackles a profound question: if God creates all actions, how can humans deserve reward or punishment? The author shows that denying human agency leads to absurd consequences that even the Quran rejects. This passage is a tight logical argument that draws out the ethical implications of predestinarian views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic text aloud if possible. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'law' (if).
The proof has a clear logical flow: premise → consequence → absurdity → Quranic refutation.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'استحقاق' (desert), 'الثواب' (reward), 'العقاب' (punishment), and 'السفهاء' (foolish). How do these terms carry the argument?
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument step by step: (1) If no human origination, no desert. (2) Without desert, God's reward/punishment is arbitrary. (3) That would make punishing prophets and rewarding tyrants possible. (4) That would make God the most foolish. (5) But the Quran treats Muslims and criminals differently, so that is false.
This chain shows why the issue matters for divine justice.
4
Reflect on the Quranic proof
Read the verse from Surah al-Qalam (68:35-36). Why does the author cite it as evidence that God rejects treating believers and criminals alike?
The verse is a rhetorical question that implies God does not act unjustly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لما استحق ثوابا ، ولكان تعالى مبتدئاً بالثواب والعقاب من غير حصول استحقاق لذلك . ولو جاز ذلك ، لجاز تعذيب الأنبياء وإثابة الفراعنة والأبالسة . ولو كان كذلك ، لكان أسفه السفهاء. وقد نزه نفسه عن أن يفعل ذلك فقال في كتابه : أفنجعل المسلمين كالمجرمين ؟ ما لكم ؟ كيف تحكمون (۳) ؟ )
The Tenth Proof: If the servant were not an originator of his own actions, then he would not deserve a reward, and He, exalted, would be the initiator of reward and punishment without any desert for that. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible to punish the prophets and reward the Pharaohs and devils. And if that were so, then He would be the most foolish of the foolish. And He has declared Himself innocent of doing that in His Book, saying: 'Shall We treat the Muslims as the criminals? What is the matter with you? How do you judge?' (Al-Qalam 68:35-36).
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage argues that if humans do not originate their own actions, they cannot deserve (استحقاق) reward or punishment. God would then be the direct initiator of reward and punishment without any basis of desert. This would open the possibility of punishing prophets and rewarding tyrants like Pharaohs and devils—an absurdity that would make God the most foolish. The author then cites a Quranic verse (68:35-36) that rhetorically asks whether Muslims and criminals are treated equally, implying that God does not act unjustly.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق
الثواب
العقاب
السفهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can reward or punish arbitrarily without any basis of desert.
Correction: The passage argues that such arbitrariness would make God foolish and is refuted by the Quran, which shows that God differentiates between Muslims and criminals.

Checkpoint
What would follow if the servant does not create his own actions, according to the tenth proof?
A) He would still deserve reward based on God's will.
B) He would not deserve reward or punishment, leading to the possibility of punishing prophets.
C) God would be forced to treat everyone equally.
D) Humans would have no moral responsibility.

Key Takeaway
The tenth proof shows that denying human agency leads to the absurd conclusion that God could punish prophets and reward tyrants, which contradicts divine justice and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though your actions originate from you, or do you sometimes shift responsibility to God’s decree or your circumstances? This lesson puts the weight of your deeds on your own shoulders. Will you accept that weight and face your actions without excuses?
?
If God’s justice is grounded in desert, then your salvation hinges on what you actually do. Are you acting today as if your choices eternally matter, or are you coasting on the assumption that God will be arbitrary and merciful regardless?
?
God asks in the Quran: 'Shall We treat the Muslims as the criminals?' How would you answer that question with your own life? If your deeds were weighed right now, would you be counted among the Muslims or the criminals? What needs to change starting today?

The Eleventh Proof: God Creating Disbelief
A conditional argument that if God creates disbelief, He creates the disbeliever for punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the eleventh proof: that if God directly creates disbelief, then He is the cause of the punishment that follows, raising questions about His justice.

Core Idea
If God creates disbelief, He creates the disbeliever for punishment.

The eleventh proof in the sequence tackles a difficult theological question: if God creates disbelief in a person, does that make God the author of the punishment that follows? This brief conditional argument sets up a challenge that theologians must address. It is a compact statement, but its implications are far-reaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once. Note its conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
The passage is very concise.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The premise is that God creates disbelief in the disbeliever. The conclusion is that He creates him for punishment.
3
Understand the implication
If the conclusion holds, then God directly causes punishment, which seems problematic for divine justice.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the problem posed
Consider why theologians would find this conclusion undesirable. It challenges the idea that humans are responsible for their own disbelief.
This connects to broader debates on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : لو خلق الكفر في الكافر، لكان قد خلقه للعذاب
The Eleventh Proof: If He created disbelief in the disbeliever, then He would have created him for punishment.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a very brief statement of the eleventh proof. It points out that if God creates disbelief in a disbeliever, then the purpose of creating that disbelief would be to bring about punishment. The implication is that this would make God the direct cause of the punishment for something He Himself created, which raises questions about justice. The argument is likely expanded further in the original text, but as presented here, it is a concise conditional leading to an undesirable conclusion.

Key Terms
خلق الكفر
العذاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second part of the conditional (the then-clause)?
A) God creates disbelief
B) God creates the disbeliever for punishment
C) God is just
D) God is omnipotent

Key Takeaway
The eleventh proof uses a conditional to argue that if God creates disbelief, He becomes the direct cause of punishment, which is a problem for divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is the creator of all things, including disbelief, how do you personally reconcile that with the existence of punishment for disbelief? Does this conditional argument unsettle your trust in God's justice? What would it mean for your own faith if this problem had no answer?
?
Consider a time when you felt unfairly judged. This lesson presents a logical dilemma that, if unresolved, could make God appear unjust. How does this challenge you to deepen your understanding of God's wisdom and mercy? What concrete step will you take to seek answers to such theological problems?

The Seventh Proof: God's Wisdom Precludes Creating Evil Actions
Lists evil servant actions and argues God cannot create them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God's wisdom necessarily prevents Him from creating evil actions like shirk and blasphemy.

Core Idea
A wise God cannot create actions that insult Himself.

This lesson presents a key argument from the Ash'ari-Maturidi debates on qadar. The author lists specific evil acts committed by some humans—shirk, describing God with imperfections, claiming He has offspring, and reviling Him—and then argues that if God were the creator of all actions, He would be the creator of these insults. Since that is incompatible with His perfect wisdom, God cannot be the creator of such acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the list of actions and the logical connector 'فلو كان'.
2
Identify the enumerated evil actions
List out the four specific actions mentioned: shirk, describing God with opposites, claiming consort/children, and reviling/insulting. Why are these chosen?
They represent the worst forms of human transgression against God.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The argument is a reductio: if God created all actions, then He would create these evils. But that would nullify His wisdom because a wise being does not insult Himself. Trace the logic step by step.
The conclusion is implicit: therefore God does not create all actions.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider what this means for human responsibility. If God does not create evil acts, then humans must be the agents. How does this fit with divine omnipotence?
This is a classic argument for human free will in the context of qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن من جملة أفعال العباد : الإشراك بالله ، ووصفه بالأضداد ، والصاحبة والأولاد ، وشتمه وسبه . فلو كان تعالى فاعلاً لأفعال العباد ، لكان فاعلاً لكل هذه الأمور ، وذلك يبطل حكمته . لأن الحكيم لا يشتم نفسه . وفي ذلك إلحاقه بالسفهاء .
The seventh proof: Indeed, among the actions of the servants are associating partners with Allah, describing Him with opposites, (claiming) a consort and children, and reviling and insulting Him. So if the Exalted were the doer of the actions of the servants, He would be the doer of all these things, and that would nullify His wisdom. Because the wise does not revile himself. And in that is likening Him to the foolish.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) from the category of reductio ad absurdum against the view that God creates all human actions. It begins by listing specific evil actions performed by some servants: shirk (associating partners with God), describing God with opposites (attributing imperfection), claiming He has a consort or children, and reviling and insulting Him. The argument then states that if God were the creator of all actions of servants, He would be the creator of these evil actions as well. But that would nullify God's wisdom (ḥikma), because a wise being does not revile himself. Furthermore, attributing such actions to God would liken Him to the foolish (sufahā'). The implication is that since God is necessarily wise and free from foolishness, He cannot be the creator of these evil acts. Therefore, the actions must originate from the servants themselves, not from God's creative power.

Key Terms
أفعال العباد
حكمته
السفهاء
الإشراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument implies God is unable to create evil actions.
Correction: It is not about inability but about logical incompatibility: a wise being cannot create actions that insult Himself because that contradicts His attribute of wisdom.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh proof use as evidence that God does not create all actions of servants?
A) God's mercy
B) God's wisdom requires that He not create actions that insult Himself
C) God's power is limited
D) The servants are compelled to act

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom logically excludes the creation of actions that defy His own majesty, thus evil actions must originate from humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have sinned, have you ever caught yourself thinking, 'It was written for me,' as a way to avoid full responsibility? How does this proof dismantle that excuse? What specific step will you take today to own your choices before Allah?
?
You hear others using qadar to justify their sins. Does this passage allow you to still see them as victims of fate? Or does it demand you hold them—and yourself—accountable? Which sin will you stop blaming on Allah's decree starting now?
?
If Allah's wisdom is so perfect that He cannot create self-insulting actions, what does that say about the moral weight of your every deed? Are you living as if your actions truly matter in shaping your character? What one action will you change tomorrow to reflect that belief?

The Eighth Proof: Absurd Consequences of God Creating Immoral Acts
Reductio ad absurdum argument against God creating sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reductio ad absurdum argument that if God creates immoral acts like fornication, it would imply absurd prophecies calling to evil, proving that God does not create evil actions.

Core Idea
If God creates evil, He could send prophets of evil—an absurdity.

This lesson presents the eighth proof from the text, which uses a logical chain to show the unacceptable implications of claiming that God creates all human actions, including immoral ones. By imagining the consequences, the author demonstrates the necessity of distinguishing between what God creates and what humans choose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then' repeated.
2
Identify the initial assumption
The argument begins by supposing that it is permissible for God to create fornication and sodomy. This is the starting point of the reductio.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the chain: from the first assumption, it follows that God could send a prophet whose religion is these acts. Then further, earlier prophets could be sent only to call to evil and repudiate good.
The list of absurdities includes theft, devil worship, disrespect, and reversing moral praise and blame.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the consequences are absurd and contrary to the purpose of prophethood, the original assumption must be false. Thus, God does not create immoral acts.
5
Reflect on the significance
This proof defends divine perfection and human responsibility: evil originates from human choice, not from God's creation.
This connects to broader discussions of qadar (divine decree) and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : لو جاز أن يخلق الزنا واللواطة ، لجاز أن يبعث رسولاً هذا دينه . ولو جاز ذلك ، لجاز فيما سلف من الأنبياء من لم يبعث إلا للدعوة إلى السرقة ، والأفعال القبيحة ، ومدح الشيطان وعبادته ، والاستخفاف بالله وبرسوله وبالوالدين ، وذم المحسن ومدح المسيء .
The eighth proof: If it were permissible that He create fornication and sodomy, then it would be permissible that He send a messenger whose religion is this. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible in the past among the prophets that some were not sent except to call to theft, evil actions, praising Satan and worshiping him, showing disrespect to Allah, His Messenger, and to parents, and blaming the good-doer and praising the evil-doer.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof, a reductio ad absurdum that shows the unacceptable implications of claiming God creates all human actions. It begins by supposing that God could create fornication (zinā) and sodomy (liwāṭa). If so, then He could also send a prophet whose religion is precisely these acts. Carrying this further, it would then be possible that some earlier prophets were sent only to call people to theft, evil deeds, praising and worshiping Satan, showing disrespect to God, His messenger, and parents, and reversing moral judgments (blaming the good and praising the evil). Since such a scenario is absurd—prophets are sent with guidance, not corruption—the original assumption that God creates all human actions, including immoral ones, must be false. The conclusion is that God's creation is restricted to good, and evil actions originate from human choice.

Key Terms
الزنا
اللواطة
رسولاً
الشيطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof asserts that God actually does send prophets with evil religions.
Correction: The proof does not claim God actually does such things; it shows the logical impossibility of the contrary view by reductio ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
What does the eighth proof conclude is an absurd consequence if God creates zina?
A) Prophets would call to good
B) Prophets could be sent to call to theft and devil worship
C) People would stop sinning
D) God would be unjust

Key Takeaway
God's creation is restricted to good; evil originates from human choice, otherwise prophethood would be meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is the creator of all your actions, including your sins, then how do you explain the fact that He sent prophets to forbid exactly those sins? Are you willing to accuse God of contradicting Himself? This is the very absurdity the argument exposes. What does your belief actually commit you to?
?
Consider a time you sinned. Did you attribute that sin to God's creation or to your own choice? If you attribute it to God, then you must accept that God could have sent a prophet to command that sin. Does that not terrify you? How can you claim to love a God who could command evil?
?
The proof shows that if God created evil, prophethood would be meaningless. In your life, do you treat prophethood as a guide that distinguishes good from evil, or do you sometimes blur that line by excusing your own sins as 'God's will'? Repentance requires a clear distinction—are you willing to draw that line?

The Ninth Proof: God’s Harm Would Exceed Satan’s
Compares God's creating disbelief to Satan's temptation; God's harm is greater.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing the creation of disbelief to God leads to a morally absurd conclusion.

Core Idea
If God forced disbelief, He would be more harmful than Satan — a conclusion no Muslim can accept.

This proof compares God’s hypothetical role in creating disbelief to Satan’s temptation. Satan only invites, but if God compels and then punishes, the disbeliever would be justified in thanking Satan and blaming God — an obvious absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice how the author sets up a conditional and draws an implication.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the comparison
Look for where the author compares God’s hypothetical action to Satan’s. Note the Quranic verse used to define Satan’s role.
Satan’s authority is limited to calling.
3
Reflect on the absurd conclusion
Consider why it would be ‘good’ for the disbeliever to thank Satan and revile God. This absurdity shows the premise must be false.
The conclusion is explicitly rejected with 'ta'ala allahu 'uluwwan kabira'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : لو كان تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر ، ثم يعذبه عليه لكان ضرره على العبد أشد من ضرر الشيطان [لأنه] لا يمكنه أن يلجئهم إلى القبح ، بل الغاية القصوى منه : أن يدعوهم إلى القبح . كما حكى الله تعالى ذلك عنه في قوله : ( وما كان لي عليكم من سلطان . إلا أن دعوتكم ) وأيضاً : فدعاء الشيطان أيضاً من فعل الله تعالى . وأما الله سبحانه ، فإنه يلجئهم إلى القبائح ويخلقها فيهم. ولو كان كذلك ، لكان يحسن من الكافر أن يمنح الشيطان ، وأن يذم الله سبحانه - تعالى الله علواً كبيراً . .
The ninth proof: If the Exalted were to create disbelief in the disbeliever and then punish him for it, then His harm upon the servant would be more severe than the harm of Satan, because Satan cannot compel them to evil; rather, his utmost is that he calls them to evil. As Allah the Exalted related that about him in His saying: 'And I had no authority over you except that I called you.' Also, Satan's call itself is from the act of Allah the Exalted. But Allah the Exalted, He compels them to evil and creates it in them. And if that were the case, then it would be good for the disbeliever to thank Satan and to revile Allah the Exalted—exalted is Allah, highly exalted.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is the ninth proof, comparing God's hypothetical role in creating disbelief (kufr) to Satan's role. It begins by assuming God creates kufr in the disbeliever and then punishes him for it. This would make God's harm more severe than Satan's, because Satan cannot force anyone into evil; his only power is to invite (da'wa), as the Quran says: 'I had no authority over you except that I called you' (Ibrahim 14:22). Moreover, even Satan's call is itself created by God, so all evil ultimately traces back to God. The argument then points out that if God compels people to commit evil and creates it in them, then it would be fitting (ḥasana) for the disbeliever to thank Satan (since Satan only invited, while God forced) and to revile God. This is an absurd conclusion, as God is infinitely exalted above such blame. Therefore, the initial premise (that God creates disbelief) is false. The proof highlights the moral repugnance of attributing coercion to God in matters of faith.

Key Terms
الكفر
الشيطان
السلطان
دعوتكم
القبائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that Satan's call is from God's creation.
Correction: The proof actually acknowledges that Satan's call is from God's creation, but it argues that God's direct creation of evil would be even worse than Satan's temptation.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, if God creates disbelief in a person and punishes him, why would the disbeliever be justified in thanking Satan?
A) Satan created the disbelief
B) Satan only called to disbelief, while God forced it
C) Satan is more merciful
D) God cannot punish

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof shows that if God created disbelief and then punished for it, He would be worse than Satan, an absurd conclusion that disproves the premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever secretly blame your circumstances or even God for 'making it easy to sin'? How does this proof confront that excuse and place full responsibility on your own choice to follow the invitation of Satan?
?
Satan only invited, and you followed. This is a stark reality. Will you stand before Allah on Judgment Day and try to deflect blame, or will you honestly say, 'I chose to disobey'? What concrete step will you take today to own your repentance and act on it?
?
If you truly believed that God compelled your disobedience and then punished you for it, would you feel gratitude or resentment toward Him? How does recognizing that God does not compel evil transform your relationship with Him—shifting from resentment to thankfulness for the gift of choice?

Tenth Proof: No Reward Without True Agency
Sanusi presents a reductio ad absurdum for the necessity of human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the tenth proof demonstrates that divine justice requires humans to be genuine agents of their actions.

Core Idea
Without human agency, reward and punishment become arbitrary and unjust.

The tenth proof tackles a central question: if God creates all actions, how can humans deserve reward or punishment? Sanusi argues that without human agency, divine reward and punishment lose moral basis. He pushes the logic to an absurd extreme and seals the argument with a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement, an absurd consequence, and a Quranic refutation.
This proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the logical chain
Break down the argument: (1) If no agency, then no desert. (2) If no desert, then God's reward/punishment is arbitrary. (3) If arbitrary, then even prophets could be punished and tyrants rewarded. (4) That would make God the most foolish, which is impossible.
The argument uses an impossible consequence to prove the premise false.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Read the verse Sanusi cites: 'Shall We treat the Muslims as the criminals?' Reflect on how it affirms that God distinguishes people based on their deeds, not arbitrarily.
The verse provides direct scriptural support for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لما استحق ثوابا ، ولكان تعالى مبتدئاً بالثواب والعقاب من غير حصول استحقاق لذلك . ولو جاز ذلك ، لجاز تعذيب الأنبياء وإثابة الفراعنة والأبالسة . ولو كان كذلك ، لكان أسفه السفهاء. وقد نزه نفسه عن أن يفعل ذلك فقال في كتابه : أفنجعل المسلمين كالمجرمين ؟ ما لكم ؟ كيف تحكمون ؟
The tenth proof: If the servant were not the originator of his own actions, he would not deserve reward, and the Exalted would be initiating reward and punishment without any deservingness for that. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible to punish the prophets and reward the Pharaohs and devils. And if that were the case, then He would be the most foolish of the foolish. And He has declared Himself free from doing that in His Book: 'Shall We treat the Muslims as the criminals? What is the matter with you? How do you judge?'
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is the tenth proof, focusing on the necessity of human agency for moral accountability. It begins by stating that if the servant (ʿabd) is not the originator (mūjid) of his own actions, then he does not deserve reward or punishment. In that case, God would be arbitrarily bestowing reward and punishment without any basis of deservingness (istiḥqāq). If that were allowed, it would be permissible for God to punish the prophets and reward tyrants like Pharaoh and the devils—clearly absurd. The argument concludes that such a being would be the most foolish (asfah al-sufahā'), which is impossible for God. The proof then cites the Quranic verse: 'Shall We treat the Muslims as the criminals? What is the matter with you? How do you judge?' (al-Qalam 68:35-36), which affirms that God treats people according to their deeds, not arbitrarily. Thus, the servant must have genuine agency for divine justice to be meaningful.

استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies God's absolute power over creation.
Correction: The proof does not deny God's power; it affirms that His justice requires human agency as the basis for reward and punishment.

D) The Quran would be wrong

Key Takeaway

God's Blessings and Eternal Punishment
Argues that creating kufr for eternal punishment negates God's blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eleventh proof argues that if God creates disbelief and punishes eternally, then His blessings are negated, and why this supports the view that disbelief is not created by God.

Core Idea
God's true blessings cannot coexist with eternal punishment for disbelief.

This proof tackles a deep theological question: If God creates disbelief, can He still be considered a source of blessing for the disbeliever? The passage argues that eternal punishment outweighs any temporary pleasure, rendering God's blessings meaningless. Through a vivid analogy and Quranic references, the author shows the logical inconsistency of creating disbelief for eternal punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the conditional structure and the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage begins with 'If He created kufr... then He would have created him for punishment.'
2
Understand the two consequences
Identify the two consequences: first, God would not be a blessing in religion; second, worldly blessings would also be negated. Understand the reasoning behind each.
3
Analyze the analogy
Examine the analogy of poison in honey. Why does this illustrate that temporary pleasure is not a blessing when eternal punishment follows?
The analogy is central to the proof's argument about the insignificance of worldly joys.
4
Reflect on the Quranic references
Read the two cited verses. How do they support the conclusion that insisting on God creating disbelief contradicts the Quran?
The first verse (14:28) speaks of exchanging blessings for disbelief, implying human choice.
5
Grasp the overall argument
Synthesize the proof: if God creates disbelief for eternal punishment, then no true blessings exist, which conflicts with the Quranic portrayal of God's goodness.
This proof opposes the view that God creates all acts, including disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : لو خلق الكفر في الكافر، لكان قد خلقه للعذاب في نار جهنم . ولو كان كذلك، لما كان الله نعمة على الكافر أصلا في الدين وأيضاً : على هذا التقدير، وجب أن لا يكون له عليه شيء من النعم الدنيوية، لأن اللذات العاجلة بالنسبة إلى العقوبة الأبدية . كالقطرة في البحر . وذلك لا يعد نعمة . ألا ترى أن من جعل السم في الحلو، فإن اللذة الحاصلة من تناول ذلك [الحلو] لا تعد نعمة. فكذا ها هنا . ومتي التزم ملتزم فقد خالف الكتاب . قال تعالى : ( ألم تر إلى الذين بدلوا نعمة الله كفراً ) وقال : ( وأحسن كما أحسن الله إليك )
The eleventh proof: If He created disbelief in the disbeliever, He would have created him for punishment in the fire of Hell. And if that were the case, then Allah would not be a blessing upon the disbeliever at all in religion. Also, upon this assumption, it would be necessary that there would be no worldly blessings from Him upon him, because the immediate pleasures in comparison to the eternal punishment are like a drop in the ocean. And that is not considered a blessing. Do you not see that whoever puts poison in honey, the pleasure obtained from consuming that honey is not considered a blessing? Likewise here. And whoever insists (on this view) has contradicted the Book. He said: 'Have you not seen those who exchanged the blessing of Allah for disbelief?' And He said: 'And do good as Allah has done good to you.'
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is the eleventh proof, focusing on the concept of divine blessings (niʿma) and eternal punishment. It starts by assuming God creates disbelief in the disbeliever and then punishes him eternally in Hell. On this view, God would not be a blessing to the disbeliever in religion at all. Moreover, even worldly pleasures would cease to be true blessings, because compared to eternal punishment, they are negligible—like a drop in the ocean. The proof uses an analogy: if someone puts poison in honey, the pleasure from eating that honey is not considered a blessing because it leads to death. Similarly, if God creates disbelief and then punishes eternally, any temporary worldly enjoyment is negated. The argument concludes that anyone who insists on this view has contradicted the Quran, citing two verses: 'Have you not seen those who exchanged the blessing of Allah for disbelief?' (Ibrahim 14:28) and 'And do good as Allah has done good to you' (al-Qasas 28:77). The former shows that blessings are exchanged for disbelief by human choice, and the latter implies God's goodness is a model for human goodness, not a source of compulsion.

Correction: The proof does not deny worldly pleasures; it argues that these pleasures are not true blessings when they lead to eternal punishment.

Divine Names Follow Actions: The Twelfth Proof
Argues that divine names derive from actions; God's perfection precludes injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the semantic argument that God's names are derived from His actual actions, and therefore He cannot be called oppressor since He never creates injustice.

Core Idea
Names follow actions; since God does no injustice, He is not an oppressor.

This twelfth proof tackles a key semantic point: if God were to create injustice, tyranny, and corruption, then the names 'oppressor', 'tyrant', and 'corrupter' would rightfully apply to Him. But because in Arabic names are grounded in actual deeds, and God's acts are perfectly just, these names cannot be used. Let's unpack the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the conditional structure and the conclusion.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical flow from hypothesis to result.
2
Identify the main conditional statement
Find the 'if...then' part: 'If He created injustice, tyranny, and corruption, then describing Him as an oppressor... would be valid.' Underline this.
3
Understand the semantic principle
The proof states that the meaning of 'oppressor' is simply 'doer of injustice'. Names derive from actions. This principle is crucial.
This applies to all names: a person is called 'just' only if they do justice.
4
Apply the principle to God
Since God does not commit any injustice, He cannot be called oppressor. The proof concludes that He must be called just, and the opposite is impossible.
This refutes the idea that God could create evil and still be called just arbitrarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : لو خلق الظلم والجور والفساد، تصح وصفه بأنه ظالم وجائر ومفسد، لأنه لا معنى للظالم إلا كونه فاعلاً للظلم . ولذلك فإنه لا يصح إثبات سمي عادلاً، فكذا لو ظالماً وسفيها، يسمى العبد أحدهما مع نفي الآخر . وأيضاً : فإنه لما فعل العدل فعل الظلم وجب أن يسمى ظالماً، بل يجب أن لا لأنه لم يفعل شيئا من ذلك .
The twelfth proof: If He created injustice, tyranny, and corruption, then describing Him as an oppressor, tyrant, and corrupter would be valid, because the meaning of 'oppressor' is nothing other than being a doer of injustice. Therefore, it is not valid to affirm that He is called just while also (being) an oppressor and foolish; a servant is called one or the other while negating the other. Also, if He did justice and then did injustice, He would necessarily be called an oppressor, but rather it is necessary that He not (be called oppressor) because He has not done anything of that.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is the twelfth proof, focusing on the semantic implications of attributing actions to God. It begins by stating that if God created injustice (ẓulm), tyranny (jawr), and corruption (fasād), then it would be valid to describe Him as an oppressor (ẓālim), tyrant (jā'ir), and corrupter (mufsid). This is because the meaning of 'oppressor' is simply being a doer of injustice. Consequently, it is not valid to affirm that He is called just (ʿādil) if He is also an oppressor and foolish; a servant is called either just or unjust, not both. The argument further says that if God did justice and then did injustice, He would necessarily be called an oppressor. However, the opposite is true: He must not be called oppressor because He has not done any injustice. The reasoning is that names derive from the actual occurrence of actions; since God does not commit injustice, He is not called oppressor. Therefore, claiming God creates injustice contradicts His attribute of justice.

Key Terms
الظلم
الجور
الفساد
ظالم
عادلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could create injustice and still be called just because He is the Creator and can define names arbitrarily.
Correction: The proof shows that names are grounded in actions; if God created injustice, He would necessarily be called oppressor, contradicting His justice. Thus He does not create injustice.

Checkpoint
According to the twelfth proof, why is God not called an oppressor?
A) God cannot do injustice
B) The name 'oppressor' is only applied if the action occurs, and God does not do injustice
C) God changes His name arbitrarily
D) The Quran does not call God 'oppressor'

Key Takeaway
The twelfth proof establishes that God's names are not arbitrary but based on His actual attributes and actions; He is called just because He only does justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You call yourself a believer, but what name do your actions actually earn you? Are there hidden injustices in your life—in your business, your family, your heart—that contradict the justice you claim to love? Will you root them out today?
?
If God's names are based on His actual acts, your reputation among people is also built on your actual behavior. Whose name would you rather defend: your comfortable self-image or the truth of your deeds? Are you willing to let go of a false reputation?
?
The proof says a servant is called oppressor or just, negating the other. In the areas where you have power—over your children, employees, or spouse—which name do you actually deserve? Can you bear to find out? Change begins with honest naming.

The Thirteenth Proof: Absurdities of Commanding the Impossible
Sanusi presents the thirteenth proof using reductio ad absurdum analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the thirteenth proof uses absurd analogies to refute the claim that God creates disbelief in a person while commanding them not to disbelieve.

Core Idea
If God creates kufr, He cannot forbid it—that would be commanding the impossible.

This proof challenges a theological dilemma: If God creates disbelief in a person, can He then command that person not to disbelieve? The author presents a series of striking analogies to show the absurdity of such a scenario. By comparing it to tying someone and throwing them off a mountain while ordering them to return, he demonstrates that divine commands cannot contradict divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the proof begins with a supposition: 'If it were permissible that He create disbelief in the disbeliever and then say to him: Do not disbelieve.'
This is the premise being refuted.
2
Examine each analogy for impossibility
The proof lists several analogies: tying and throwing from a mountain, commanding a blind person to dot the Quran, commanding to create non-existence, and combining black and white. For each, consider why it is impossible.
These analogies highlight different aspects of impossibility—physical, sensory, logical.
3
Understand the conclusion
The proof concludes that if such impossible commands were allowed, even inanimate objects could be commanded. Since all these are necessarily false, the original premise is false.
The conclusion is based on intuitive reason (ḍarūra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر : لو جاز أن يخلق الكفر في الكافر، ثم يقول له : لا تكفر . الجاز أن يقيد يديه ورجليه ثم يرميه من شاهق الجبل، ويقول له : ارجع . ولجاز تكليف الأعمى بنقط المصحف . بل يلزم جواز التكليف بخلق العدم، والجمع بين السواد والبياض . ولو جاز ذلك، لجاز تكليف الجمادات . وكل ذلك باطل بالضرورة .
The thirteenth proof: If it were permissible that He create disbelief in the disbeliever and then say to him: 'Do not disbelieve,' then it would be permissible that He tie his hands and legs and then throw him from a high mountain and say to him: 'Return.' And it would be permissible to charge a blind person with dotting the Qur'an. Rather, it would necessitate the permissibility of charging (someone) with creating non-existence and combining black and white. And if that were permissible, then charging inanimate objects would be permissible. And all of that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is the thirteenth proof, using absurd examples to show the impossibility of God creating disbelief in a person while commanding him not to disbelieve. It begins by supposing that God creates kufr in the disbeliever and then prohibits it. This is likened to tying someone's hands and feet, throwing him from a high mountain, and ordering him to return—an impossible command. Similarly, it would be like commanding a blind person to dot the letters of the Quran (which requires sight). More broadly, it would allow commands to create non-existence (which is impossible) and to combine contradictory attributes (like black and white in the same subject). Ultimately, if such impossible commands were allowed, even inanimate objects could be commanded. Since all these are necessarily false by intuitive reason (ḍarūra), the original premise that God creates disbelief and then prohibits it must be false. The proof highlights that divine commands must be consistent with human capacity; if God creates an action, He cannot simultaneously forbid it, as that would be a contradiction.

Key Terms
تكليف
الأعمى
العدم
السواد والبياض
الجمادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that God has the power to command anything, or that He could create disbelief and still forbid it.
Correction: The proof does not deny God's ability to command; it denies that He would command what He has already decreed, as that would be logically absurd and inconsistent with His wisdom.

Checkpoint
What does the thirteenth proof compare to God creating disbelief and then forbidding it?
A) Commanding a bird to fly
B) Tying a person and throwing him off a mountain then ordering him to return
C) Making a rich man poor
D) Forbidding what is already done

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that commanding the impossible (e.g., forbidding what God has created) is necessarily absurd, thus affirming that God's commands are consistent with His decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses for a sin you know God has forbidden, do you truly believe His command is possible for you, or have you silently deemed it impossible? Will you today identify one such command and take a concrete step to obey it, trusting that God does not command what you cannot do?
?
If God were to create disbelief in you and then forbid it, you would have no way to escape punishment. How does this proof strengthen your conviction that you are fully responsible for your choices? Are there areas where you blame Allah or your circumstances for your failures instead of owning them?
?
The proof shows that commanding the impossible is necessarily false. Consider a command of Allah you have ignored because it felt too hard. What would change if you embraced the certainty that it is possible for you? Will you commit today to act on that command as if it were the most natural thing, knowing that God never requires the impossible?

The Fourteenth Proof: A Dilemma on Human Agency
A disjunctive dilemma refuting exclusive divine creation of human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourteenth proof uses a logical dilemma to argue that the view of God creating all human acts leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Human acts cannot be fully created by God without undermining human skill and intention.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate over human free will and divine creation. The author constructs a dilemma: either God's creation of human actions depends on human motives and power, or it does not. Both options lead to unacceptable conclusions. By examining this proof, we see how rational argumentation challenges the idea that God is the sole efficient cause of every human act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument structure come through.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The proof presents a conditional with two alternatives. Find the two possibilities and note how each is introduced.
Look for the phrases 'إما أن يتوقف' (either depends) and 'أو لا يتوقف' (or does not depend).
3
Examine the first branch
The first option leads to God being in need of human will and power. Understand why that is considered invalid from a theological perspective.
The impossibility of God being dependent (muḥtāj) is a core theological principle.
4
Examine the second branch
The second option leads to the absurd consequence that skilled acts could occur without knowledge. Reflect on why that is impossible.
The example of writing and craftsmanship is used to illustrate this absurdity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both branches are false, the original premise (God creating all human acts) must be false. Consider what this implies about human agency.
This proof supports the view that humans have genuine agency and are not mere puppets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشر : لو كان تعالى هو الخالق لأعمال العباد، لكان إماً أن يتوقف خلقه لها على دواعيهم وقدرهم، أو لا يتوقف . والأول باطل . لأنه يلزم منه أن يكون تعالي محتاجاً إلى إرادتهم وقدرتهم . والثاني باطل لأنه يلزم أن منه تعالى خلق تلك الأفعال من دون قدرتهم وإرادتهم . فيلزم صحة أن توجد الكتابة والصياغة المحكمتان فيمن لا يكون عالماً بالصياغة والكتابة، يصح .
The fourteenth proof: If the Exalted were the creator of the actions of the servants, then either His creation of them depends on their motives and power, or it does not depend. The first is invalid because it would imply that the Exalted is in need of their will and power. The second is invalid because it would imply that the Exalted creates those actions without their power and will. Then it would necessarily be valid that well-crafted writing and craftsmanship exist in someone who has no knowledge of craftsmanship and writing; it is valid.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is the fourteenth proof, a disjunctive dilemma targeting the claim that God is the sole creator of human actions. It presents two possibilities: either God's creation of human acts depends on the human's motives (dawāʿī) and power (qudar), or it does not. The first option is invalid because it would make God dependent (muḥtāj) on human will and ability—an imperfection incompatible with divinity. The second option is invalid because it would mean God creates acts wholly independently of human power and will, leading to the absurd conclusion that well-crafted writing (kitāba) and craftsmanship (ṣiyāgha) could appear in a person who has no knowledge of them. Since such a result is false—craftsmanship requires knowledge—the original premise (that God creates all human acts) must be false. The proof underscores that human actions require a degree of human agency and capacity; otherwise, they could occur without any corresponding skill or intention, which is contrary to experience.

Key Terms
دواعيهم
قدرهم
إرادتهم
الكتابة
الصياغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies God's power over human actions.
Correction: The proof does not deny God's ultimate power; it shows that the causal relationship between human intention and action is necessary for meaningful agency.

Checkpoint
What does the fourteenth proof argue is invalid about the second option that God creates acts independently of human power?
A) It would make God powerful
B) It would allow skilled acts to occur without knowledge
C) It would deny human free will
D) It would contradict the Quran

Key Takeaway
The fourteenth proof shows that attributing all human acts solely to divine creation leads to absurdities, requiring acknowledgment of human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your actions are entirely your own doing, or entirely from God? How does the logical dilemma in this passage force you to examine that balance in your daily life — especially in moments of success or failure?
?
When you perform a good deed, who do you credit — yourself or God? If you truly believed that all your actions were solely created by God, would you still feel responsible for your sins? This passage shows the absurdity of that view; how does it challenge you to take ownership of your choices while still relying on God?
?
Think of a skill or ability you have, like writing or speaking. If you knew that another person could produce the exact same work without any knowledge, would you feel your effort was meaningless? The proof uses this to show that human agency is real. In your prayer life, do you act as if your intentions matter to God, or do you passively expect Him to do everything?

The Twelfth Proof: God Cannot Create Injustice
Presents a reductio argument that God cannot create injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument proving that God cannot create injustice, because doing so would make Him unjust, contradicting His essential justice.

Core Idea
If God created injustice, He would be called unjust—but God is just and cannot be unjust.

This lesson unpacks the twelfth proof, which uses a logical reductio to defend God's justice. The argument starts from the opponent's assumption that God creates injustice and shows it leads to a contradiction with God's description as just. Understanding this proof helps clarify how kalam reasoning uses language and logical consistency to preserve divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: it begins with 'if He created…' then draws the consequence, and ends with a contradiction.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the logic.
2
Identify the reductio structure
Look for the assumption (God creating injustice), the consequence (He would be called unjust), the contradiction (He is called just), and the conclusion (He cannot create injustice).
Reductio ad absurdum is a common kalam argument form.
3
Understand the principle of naming
The argument relies on the idea that names ('unjust') are derived from actions ('doing injustice'). Grasp that a doer of injustice cannot be called just simultaneously.
This principle is foundational: names follow actions.
4
Apply the conclusion
Recognize that this proof refutes the claim that God creates evil human actions. Reflect on why Ash'ari theologians affirm God's power while denying He does injustice.
The proof does not deny God's power, but shows His actions are always just.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر: لو خلق الظلم والجور والفساد، تصح وصفه بأنه ظالم وجائر ومفسد، لأنه لا معنى للظالم إلا كونه فاعلاً للظلم. ولذلك فإنه لا يصح إثبات سمي عادلاً، فكذا لو ظالماً وسفيها، يسمى العبد أحدهما مع نفي الآخر. وأيضاً: فإنه لما فعل العدل فعل الظلم وجب أن يسمى ظالماً، بل يجب أن لا لأنه لم يفعل شيئاً من ذلك.
The twelfth proof: If He created injustice, tyranny, and corruption, then describing Him as unjust, tyrannical, and corrupt would be correct, because there is no meaning to 'unjust' except being a doer of injustice. Therefore it is not correct to affirm that He is called just while also calling Him unjust and foolish; the servant is called one of them while negating the other. Also: because when He does justice and does injustice, He must be called unjust, rather it is necessary that He not be called just because He did none of that.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof, which is a reductio ad absurdum. It begins by assuming the opponent's premise: 'if God created injustice, tyranny, and corruption.' The argument states that if God did so, then He would correctly be described as unjust, tyrannical, and corrupt, because the meaning of 'unjust' is simply 'one who does injustice'. The author then points out the inconsistency: if God were both just (as He is) and also did injustice, He would have to be called both just and unjust, which is contradictory. Furthermore, since God has only done justice (not injustice), He is called just and not unjust. Therefore, He cannot create injustice. This argument relies on the principle that names follow actions: a doer of injustice cannot be called just. The conclusion is that the original premise (that God creates evil human actions) is false.

Key Terms
ظلم
عدل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is all-powerful, He could create injustice, and then He would still be considered just because He is God.
Correction: The argument shows that descriptive names follow actions. If God did injustice, He would be called unjust, which is incompatible with His being just. Thus, He cannot do injustice, though He is all-powerful.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the twelfth proof?
A) God cannot create injustice because He would then be unjust.
B) God can create both justice and injustice.
C) The servant creates his own actions.
D) Injustice is not possible for God because He is all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
God cannot create injustice because doing so would make Him unjust, contradicting His essential justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This argument shows that names follow actions. If you claim to be a believer, do your actions consistently align with that name? What is one action you will change this week to make your deeds match your claim?
?
If God cannot create injustice, then every injustice you see or experience comes from humans, not from Him. How can you respond to injustice in a way that reflects trust in God's justice, rather than blaming Him?

The Thirteenth Proof: Absurdity of Commanding Disbelief While Creating It
A reductio showing contradiction in God creating disbelief and forbidding it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the thirteenth proof uses reductio ad absurdum to demonstrate the contradiction in God creating disbelief in a person while forbidding it.

Core Idea
God cannot command the impossible while creating its cause.

This lesson examines a powerful reductio argument from the Kalam tradition. The argument shows that if it were possible for God to create disbelief in a person and then forbid that very disbelief, then analogous impossibilities would follow—like tying someone's hands and feet, throwing them off a cliff, and ordering them to return. By tracing these absurd consequences, the proof demonstrates the original proposition's falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once aloud or silently. Notice its concise, logical structure. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
The argument moves from a specific example to general absurdities.
2
Identify the analogies
The author gives three analogies: (1) binding and throwing off a mountain, (2) commanding a blind man to dot a manuscript, and (3) commanding the creation of non-existence or combining black and white. List each analogy and ask what makes it impossible.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument is a reductio: if the premise (God creates kufr and forbids it) were true, then all these impossible commands would be possible. Since they are necessarily impossible, the premise is false. Trace the 'if…then' chain.
The conclusion is that the original premise is false by necessity.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
This proof defends divine wisdom and justice. It shows that God's commands are always possible for the commanded person to fulfill, otherwise the command would be absurd. Consider why this matters for understanding qadar (divine decree).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر: لو جاز أن يخلق الكفر في الكافر، ثم يقول له: لا تكفر. الجاز أن يقيد يديه ورجليه ثم يرميه من شاهق الجبل، ويقول له: ارجع. ولجاز تكليف الأعمى بنقط المصحف. بل يلزم جواز التكليف بخلق العدم، والجمع بين السواد والبياض. ولو جاز ذلك، لجاز تكليف الجمادات. وكل ذلك باطل بالضرورة.
The thirteenth proof: If it were permissible that He creates disbelief in the disbeliever, then says to him: 'Do not disbelieve' — then it would be permissible that He bind his hands and feet, then throw him from a high mountain, and say to him: 'Return.' And it would be permissible to charge the blind man with dotting the Qur'an. Rather, it would necessitate the permissibility of commanding the creation of non-existence, and the combination of black and white. And if that were permissible, then commanding inanimate objects would be permissible. All of that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth proof, which uses analogies to show the absurdity of God creating disbelief in a person and then commanding him not to disbelieve. The first analogy: it is like tying someone's hands and feet, throwing him from a high mountain, and then ordering him to come back — an impossible command. Similarly, commanding a blind man to dot the Qur'an (i.e., add diacritical marks) is impossible because he cannot see. The argument then generalizes: if such impossible commands were allowed, it would also be permissible to command the creation of non-existence (a contradiction) or to combine black and white (also impossible). Ultimately, if this were allowed, one could even command inanimate objects, which is absurd. All these consequences are false by necessity, so the original premise (that God creates disbelief and then forbids it) must be false.

Key Terms
كفر
تكليف
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof argues that God is unable to create disbelief. In reality, it argues that commanding the impossible contradicts divine wisdom and justice.
Correction: The proof does not deny God's power; it denies the coherence of commanding the impossible while causing it. The absurdity lies in the command, not in God's ability.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the thirteenth proof use to show the absurdity of God creating disbelief and then forbidding it?
A) A teacher teaching a lesson and then forbidding studying.
B) Tying someone's hands and feet, throwing them off a mountain, and ordering them to return.
C) A king forbidding theft while providing the means to steal.
D) A father commanding his son to be silent while making noise.

Key Takeaway
The thirteenth proof demonstrates that God cannot create the impossibility of obedience while commanding obedience, as that would lead to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your disobedience by blaming your circumstances or nature, forgetting that God only commands what He also empowers you to do? What specific sin will you confront today, rejecting the excuse that it was beyond your control?
?
When you choose to sin, you imply that God has created the obstacle that makes obedience impossible. Is that belief lurking in your heart? Will you repent tonight for any thought that shifts blame from your own desire to God's decree?
?
The blind man cannot dot the Qur'an, but you have sight and strength. Which command of God have you been ignoring, claiming it is too hard? Will you commit right now to obey it, trusting that God gives the ability with the command?

The Dilemma of Divine Creation of Actions
A dilemma argument about God's creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma argument against the claim that God directly creates human actions, and why both horns lead to unacceptable consequences.

Core Idea
If God creates all actions directly, absurd consequences follow from either horn of the dilemma.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument known as a dilemma (shakk). It targets the claim that God is the direct creator of human actions. By examining two possibilities and showing both lead to impossible conclusions, the argument demonstrates the necessity of human agency. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dilemma structure: 'either... or...'.
2
Identify the two horns
The passage presents two possibilities: either God's creation depends on human motives and power, or it does not. Identify each horn and note how they are rejected.
The first horn is rejected because it implies God's need, the second because it leads to absurdities.
3
Analyze the absurd consequences
The second horn lists several absurdities. List them and ask yourself why each is considered impossible. This helps internalize the argument's strength.
Examples: writing without knowledge or tools, ant moving mountains, etc.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The passage concludes that all these consequences are false by necessity. Understand why this forces the rejection of the original premise.
The argument does not deny God's power but affirms human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشر: لو كان تعالى هو الخالق لأعمال العباد، لكان إماً أن يتوقف خلقه لها على دواعيهم وقدرهم، أو لا يتوقف. والأول باطل. لأنه يلزم منه أن يكون تعالي محتاجاً إلى إرادتهم وقدرتهم. والثاني باطل لأنه يلزم أن منه تعالى خلق تلك الأفعال من دون قدرتهم وإرادتهم. فيلزم صحة أن توجد الكتابة والصياغة المحكمتان فيمن لا يكون عالماً بالصياغة والكتابة، ويلزم صحة صدور الكتابة ممن لا يد له ولا قلم. ويلزم تجويز أن تقدر النملة على نقل الجبال، وأن لا يقوى الفيل على نقل الذرة، وأن يجوز من الممنوع المقيد: المقدرة، وأن يعجز القادر الصحيح المخلي: من تحريك الأغلة. وفي زوال الفرق بين القوي والضعيف، وفساد تصرفاتهم في حبس السراق وقطاع الطريق. وكل ذلك معلوم الفساد بالضرورة.
The fourteenth proof: If the Exalted is the creator of the actions of servants, then either His creation of them depends on their motives and power, or it does not depend. The first is false because it would entail that the Exalted is in need of their will and power. The second is false because it entails that the Exalted creates those actions without their power and will. Then it would follow that it is correct that skilled writing and craftsmanship exist in one who has no knowledge of craftsmanship and writing, and it would follow that writing can issue from one who has no hand or pen. And it would necessitate permitting that the ant can move mountains, and that the elephant cannot move a speck, and that the restrained and bound person is able, and that the capable, healthy, and unhindered person is unable to move a chain. And it would lead to the disappearance of the distinction between the strong and the weak, and the corruption of their actions in imprisoning thieves and highway robbers. All of that is known to be false by necessity.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the fourteenth proof, structured as a dilemma. The premise is that if God is the creator of human actions, then either His creation of those actions depends on human motives and power, or it does not. The first horn (dependence) is rejected because it would make God need human will and power, which is impossible for the All-Sufficient. The second horn (independence) is also rejected because it leads to absurd consequences: if actions can occur without human power and knowledge, then a skilled craft (like writing) could appear from an ignorant person, a handless person could write, an ant could move mountains, an elephant could be weak, a bound person could be capable, and a free healthy person could be incapable. This would destroy all natural distinctions of ability and make all human judgments about capability invalid. Since these consequences are self-evidently false, the original premise (that God creates human actions) must be false.

Key Terms
دوافع
قدرة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God creates anything at all.
Correction: The argument specifically denies that God directly creates human actions without human agency. It does not deny God's overall creative power.

Checkpoint
What is the second horn of the dilemma in the fourteenth proof?
A) God's creation of actions depends on human motives.
B) God's creation of actions does not depend on human motives and power.
C) Human actions are created by both God and humans.
D) Human actions are not created by God at all.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that direct divine creation of all actions is impossible because it leads to self-evident absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever find yourself blaming your circumstances or even God for 'making' you do it? This lesson shows that such thinking would destroy all accountability. Right now, what is one sin you have been excusing by passive blame, and what step will you take today to own it fully?
?
The absurdity of a handless person writing or an ant moving mountains would, according to the argument, follow if actions were independent of human power. In what areas of your life are you acting as if your choices don't matter, as if God will just override your will? What concrete decision will you make today to exercise the agency God has given you?
?
If God created your actions without your will, you would have no real distinction from an animal, and justice would be meaningless. Do you truly live as if you are accountable for your choices? Think of a recent excuse you made for a shortcoming—was it anchored in your will or in external factors? Will you commit to changing that pattern starting now?

The Fifteenth Proof: Skepticism About Self-Evident Truths
A reductio that if God can do evil, all knowledge might be false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifteenth proof uses a reductio argument to show that denying God's optimal justice leads to universal skepticism.

Core Idea
If God can do evil, even self-evident truths become doubtful.

This proof tackles a dangerous consequence of the opposing view. The author argues that allowing evil in God would unravel all certainty, including the very basis of knowledge. Let's see how this reductio works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise logical structure: a conditional statement followed by its implications.
The proof is short but dense; every word matters.
2
Identify the hypothetical premise
The argument begins with 'if we permit the occurrence of evil acts from God'. This is the premise that the opponent holds.
3
Trace the consequence
From that premise, the argument derives a possible consequence: our self-evident knowledge might be created as ignorance, leading to doubt in all basic truths.
This is a slippery slope from permitting evil to permitting deception.
4
Recognize the absurd conclusion
The consequence ends in skepticism about self-evident truths, which is impossible – we are compelled to accept them.
The author labels it 'false and impossible'.
5
Understand the reductio logic
Since the conclusion is absurd, the original premise must be false. Therefore, God cannot do evil. This is the core of the proof.
This proof defends the Ashʿarī doctrine of optimal justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشر: لو جوزنا صدور القبائح من الله تعالى فلعل هذه العلوم البديهية الحاصلة فينا، كلها جهالات، خلقها الله فينا، وجعلها فرية متأكدة في قلوبنا. وتجويز ذلك يفضي إلى الشك في البديهيات، وكل باطل محال.
The fifteenth proof: If we permit the occurrence of evil acts from God the Exalted, then perhaps these self-evident sciences (al-ʿulūm al-badīhiyya) present in us are all ignorances; God created them in us and made them a firmly established falsehood in our hearts. And permitting that leads to doubt about self-evident truths, and all of that is false and impossible.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth proof, which is a reductio ad absurdum. It starts by assuming the opponent's position: that God can perform evil acts (qabāʾiḥ). If that is allowed, then one could also claim that all self-evident knowledge (al-ʿulūm al-badīhiyya) that we possess — such as the impossibility of contradictions — might be mere ignorances created by God and made to seem certain in our hearts. This leads to universal skepticism, where we can doubt even the most basic truths. Since skepticism about self-evident truths is impossible (because we are compelled to accept them), the original premise that God can do evil must be rejected. The argument is a classic appeal to the impossibility of global deception by God, which would undermine all knowledge.

Key Terms
قبائح
بديهيات
جهالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God actually deceives us.
Correction: No, it only shows that if He could do evil, He could potentially deceive, which leads to an unacceptable skepticism.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of permitting evil acts from God according to the fifteenth proof?
A) It proves that God is just.
B) It leads to doubt about self-evident truths.
C) It shows that humans can be independent.
D) It confirms that God creates all actions.

Key Takeaway
The fifteenth proof shows that permitting evil in God would undermine all certainty, thus defending God's absolute justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unjust, does your soul naturally question God's goodness? How does the impossibility of universal doubt in this proof call you to reaffirm your trust in God's absolute justice?
?
If the certainty you have in self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part' is a gift from God, does your certainty in His justice match that level? What steps can you take tonight to align your heart with this proof?
?
This proof argues that God cannot deceive you about basic truths. How would your life change if you truly believed that God never deceives you—even in the midst of confusion or suffering? What decision would you make tomorrow based on that conviction?
