Answering the Third Doubt: Two Responses
The author refutes the third doubt with two answers and shows the underlying principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite doubt about commanding the impossible and how it reduces to the rejection of rational good and evil.

Core Idea
The doubt collapses when we reject rational good and evil as a basis for limiting God's command.

This passage shows how the Ash'ari school defends divine omnipotence by challenging the very foundation of the Mu'tazilite objection. The author provides two direct answers and then demonstrates that all related arguments collapse once the principle of rational morality is abandoned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two distinct responses the author gives.
2
Identify the first response
The first response refers to the ten proofs that God does command what cannot be borne. This directly refutes the doubt's premise that God does not do such things.
The ten proofs are not detailed here, but they establish the principle.
3
Identify the second response
The second response points out that the doubt relies on the doctrine of rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-'aqliyyān), which Ash'aris reject.
This is the more fundamental refutation.
4
Examine the examples
The author lists several Mu'tazilite examples (sending messengers to inanimate objects, a wise being creating reviling of Himself, impossible commands). See how they all reduce to rational judgments of ugliness.
Consider: why would these examples be considered ugly in the intellect?
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author says that if we dispute the principle of rational good and evil, all such objections collapse. This is the key takeaway.
This shows the foundational role of the doctrine in the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثالثة :
فالجواب عنها من وجهين :
الأول : إنا بينا بالدلائل العشرة : أنه تعالى كلف بما لا يطاق . وذلك
يبطل قولهم : أنه تعالى لا يفعل ذلك
والثاني : إنه بناء على الحسن والقبح العقلين . وذلك باطل على ما سيأتي تقريره إن شاء الله
واعلم : أن أكثر ما ذكروه من الوجوه راجع إلى هذا الأصل . مثل : قولهم : لو جاز تكليف العبد بما لا قدرة له عليه ، لجاز إرسال الرسل إلى الجمادات . وأيضاً : لا يجوز من الحكيم أن يخلق شتم نفسه . وأيضاً : لو جاز مثل هذا التكليف ، لجاز أن يقيد يديه ورجليه ، ويلقيه من شاهق الجبل ، ثم يضربه ، ويقول له : قف في الهواء. فإن هذه الكلمات يرجع حاصلها إلى أنها قبيحة في العقول . فإذا نازعنا في هذا الأصل ، فقد سقط الكل
And as for the third doubt, the answer to it is from two aspects: 
First: We have shown by the ten proofs that He, exalted, commands what cannot be borne. And that nullifies their statement that He, exalted, does not do that. 
Second: That it is built upon rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-'aqliyyān). And that is false, as its establishment will come, God willing. 
And know: that most of what they mention of arguments return to this principle. Such as their statement: If it were permissible for God to command a servant with what he has no power over, then it would be permissible to send messengers to inanimate objects. Also: It is not permissible for a wise being to create the reviling of Himself. Also: If such a command were permissible, it would be permissible to bind his hands and legs, throw him from a high mountain, then strike him, and say to him: Stand in the air. For these words, their net result is that they are ugly in the intellects. So if we dispute this principle, then everything collapses.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage provides the Ash'ari response to a third doubt raised by the Mu'tazilites. The doubt likely argued that God cannot command what is impossible because it would be unjust or irrational. The answer has two faces. First, the Ash'aris have already proven through the ten arguments that God does indeed command the impossible, so the claim that He does not is simply false. Second, the doubt is based on the doctrine of rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-'aqliyyān), which the Ash'aris reject. They hold that actions are not inherently good or evil; rather, good and evil are determined by divine command. The author then notes that most Mu'tazilite arguments reduce to this principle of rational morality. He gives examples: that commanding the impossible would imply sending messengers to inanimate objects, or that a wise being would not create reviling against Himself, or that it would allow absurd scenarios like binding a person and then commanding him to stand in the air. All these rely on an intellectual judgment of ugliness, which the Ash'aris do not accept as a basis for limiting divine action. If that principle is rejected, all such objections fall.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح العقليان (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-'aqliyyān)
تكليف بما لا يطاق (taklif mā lā yuṭāq)
قبيح (qabīḥ)
حكيم (ḥakīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny all morality and think God can do anything without any standard.
Correction: Ash'aris reject rational good and evil as independent standards, but they affirm that good and evil are defined by divine command. Morality is not denied; it is grounded in revelation.

Checkpoint

A) The ten proofs
B) Rational good and evil
C) The impossibility of commanding the impossible
D) The example of sending messengers to inanimate objects

Key Takeaway
The third doubt is invalid because it depends on rational good and evil, which Ash'aris reject; if that principle is removed, all related objections collapse.

The author says that most doubts reduce to rational good and evil. Can you identify a doubt you have had about Allah's decree that stems from this very principle? What will you do to uproot that doubt?

Answer to the Fourth Doubt: No Circularity
The author refutes the claim that denying human origination undermines theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response refutes the Mu'tazilite argument by exposing a circularity (dawr) and why the doubt does not threaten Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite doubt collapses into either circularity or irrelevance.

The fourth doubt claimed that without human origination, belief in God, prophethood, and the Qur'an would be impossible. This lesson shows the Ash'ari logical dismantling of that claim, revealing it as either self-defeating or irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt and response
Read the passage carefully. First, understand the Mu'tazilite claim (the fourth doubt) and then the Ash'ari response that follows.
The doubt is that without human origination, belief in God, prophethood, and Qur'an would be impossible.
2
Analyze the logical disjunction
Notice that the response presents two possibilities: either belief in these principles depends on knowing humans originate actions, or it does not. Work through each case.
The first case leads to circularity (dawr), the second case shows the doubt is irrelevant.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation is important. It shows that the Mu'tazilite argument is self-defeating and does not threaten Ash'ari theology.
This logical move is a common Ash'ari defense against objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الرابعة : وهي قولهم : ولو لم يكن العبد موجـداً لأفعال نفسه ، لما أمكن الاعتراف بالصانع وبالنبوة ويكون القرآن حقاً ، فالجواب : أن الاعتراف بهذه الأصول إن كان موقوفاً على العلم يكون العبد موجداً ، لم يكن إثبات كون العبد موجدا ، بالبناء على تلك الأصول . والا وقع الدور 1 وإن لم . يكن موقوفاً عليه ، لم يلزم من القدح في كون العبد موجداً ، القدح في تلك الأصول . والله أعلم
And as for the fourth doubt, which is their statement: If the servant were not the originator (mūjid) of his own actions, then it would not be possible to acknowledge the Maker, prophethood, and that the Qur'an is true. So the answer: Acknowledgement of these principles, if it is dependent on the knowledge that the servant is an originator, then establishing that the servant is an originator would not be built upon those principles, otherwise a circularity would occur. And if it is not dependent upon it, then raising a doubt about the servant being an originator does not necessitate raising a doubt about those principles. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage addresses a fourth doubt: the Mu'tazilites argued that if humans are not the true originators of their own actions, then belief in God, prophethood, and the Qur'an would be impossible. The Ash'ari answer is a logical disjunction. If acknowledging these principles depends on first knowing that humans originate their actions, then the Mu'tazilites cannot use those principles to prove human origination—that would be circular (they would be proving human origination by relying on principles that themselves depend on human origination). If, on the other hand, acknowledgment of those principles does not depend on human origination, then even if one denies human origination, those principles remain unaffected. Therefore, the doubt is not valid. The author concludes with 'wa Allah a'lam' (And God knows best).

Key Terms
موجد (mūjid)
أصول (uṣūl)
دور (dawr / circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite doubt shows that denying human origination logically leads to rejecting God, prophethood, and the Qur'an.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that the doubt commits a circularity if it depends on human origination, or is irrelevant if it does not. Thus, denying human origination does not necessarily undermine those principles.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the author identify in the fourth doubt?
A) It leads to an infinite regress
B) It commits a circularity (dawr) if the principles depend on human origination
C) It assumes the Qur'an is false
D) It denies the existence of a Maker

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response shows that the Mu'tazilite doubt is either circular or irrelevant, thus denying human origination does not undermine belief in God, prophethood, or the Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself thinking, 'If I truly believed in qadar, I wouldn't be motivated to do good'? How does this passage's logical approach challenge the assumption that one belief necessarily undermines another? Will you examine other doubts in your faith using this same method of checking dependencies?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought that without human origination, belief in God would crumble. Is there any belief you hold that, if taken away, would make you question God's existence? What would it take for you to trust that God's existence does not depend on that belief? Are you willing to reassess the foundations of your faith?

The Purpose of Revealed Books and Their Refutation of Jabr
Classification of revealed book contents and declaration of jabr's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of revealed book contents into three categories serves as a fundamental refutation of the doctrine of jabr.

Core Idea
The contents of all revealed books inherently oppose determinism.

The author begins by summarizing the universal purposes of all revealed scriptures. He asserts that whether they contain proofs, commands, or stories, all these elements are incompatible with the doctrine of jabr (determinism). This lesson establishes the classification and the central claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage once and identify the three categories of revealed book contents: proofs, commands/prohibitions with rewards/punishments, and stories for reflection.
Notice that the author calls these 'the universal bonds and original demands'.
2
Focus on the claim that all nullify jabr
After listing the categories, the author declares that all of them nullify the doctrine of jabr. Reflect on why this classification is important for the argument.
3
Consider the relevance of the classification
Think about how each category presupposes human responsibility and choice, which is precisely what jabr denies.
This sets the stage for the detailed arguments in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنوع الأول الاستحلال الإجمالي وتقريره : إن المقصود من جميع كتب الله المنزلة . إما تقرير الدلائل على التوحيد والنبوة والمعاد . وإما الأمر والنهي، والمدح والذم ، والثواب والعقاب وإما القصص لإرادة أن يعتبر المكلف ، فيحترز عن مخالفة تكاليف الله تعالى . فهذه هي المعاقد الكلية ، والمطالب الأصلية من كتب الله المنزلة . وكلها مبطلة للقول بالجبر . أما تقرير الدلائل على التوحيد والنبوة والمعاد وسائر المطالب ، فالكل على مذهب الجبر عبث باطل . لأنه تعالى إن كان قد خلق العلم والمعرفة بتلك الأشياء ، فلا حاجة به البتة الى معرفة الدلائل . وإن كان الله لم يخلق العلم في المكلف ، لم ينتفع المكلف البتة بشيء من تلك الدلائل فعلى كلا التقديرين يكون نصب الدلائل وتقريرها وإيضاحها : عبث عبثاً ضائعاً ، على مذهب الجبر . وأما الأمر والنهي فهو أيضاً ضائع على مذهب الجبر . لأن الأمر والنهي إنما يحسنان إذا كان المأمور والمنهي قادراً على الفعل متمكناً منه . ألا ترى أن من قيد يداه ورجلاه ورمي من شاهق جبل ، ثم أمر بالرجوع، أو نهي عن النزول : كان ذلك سفهاً وعبثاً . ولو كان كل فعل : خلقه الله تعالى ، لكان جميع تكاليف الله تعالى كذلك . لأن الذي خلقه
The first type: general consideration of rendering [divine commands] void, and its explanation: The purpose of all revealed books of God is either the establishment of proofs for tawhid (divine unity), prophethood, and the afterlife, or command and prohibition, praise and blame, reward and punishment, or the stories, so that the accountable person may take heed and guard against violating the obligations of God Most High. So these are the universal bonds and the original demands from the revealed books of God. And all of them nullify the doctrine of jabr (compulsion/determinism). As for the establishment of proofs for tawhid, prophethood, the afterlife, and the remaining demands, all of it, according to the madhhab (school) of jabr, is vain and false. Because if He Most High has created knowledge and cognition of those things, then there is absolutely no need for knowledge of the proofs. And if God did not create knowledge in the accountable person, the accountable person would benefit absolutely nothing from any of those proofs. So on both suppositions, the setting up of proofs, their establishment, and their clarification is futile, utterly wasted, according to the madhhab of jabr. And as for command and prohibition, it is also wasted according to the madhhab of jabr. Because command and prohibition are only good if the one commanded and forbidden is capable of and able to perform the action. Do you not see that one whose hands and feet are tied and who is thrown from a high mountain, then is commanded to return or forbidden from descending? That would be foolishness and absurdity. And if every action were created by God Most High, then all obligations of God Most High would be like that. Because the one who created it...
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the doctrine of jabr (determinism) based on the contents of revealed books. The author first categorizes the purposes of all revealed books into three types: (1) establishing proofs for tawhid, prophethood, and the afterlife; (2) commands and prohibitions along with praise, blame, reward, and punishment; and (3) stories intended for moral reflection. These are described as the universal bonds and original demands of revelation. The author then asserts that all of these are invalidated by the doctrine of jabr. For the first type, the author presents a dilemma: if God has already created knowledge of these matters, then the proofs are unnecessary; if God does not create knowledge, then the proofs are of no benefit. Either way, setting up proofs is futile. For the second type, commands and prohibitions, the author argues that they are only meaningful if the subject is capable of performing or refraining from the action. An analogy is given: a person bound and thrown from a mountain cannot be commanded to return or forbidden from descending without absurdity. If all actions are directly created by God, then human obligation becomes similarly absurd. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the implied conclusion is that jabr leads to the invalidation of all divine commands and revelation.

Key Terms
الجبر (jabr)
التوحيد (tawhid)
النبوة (prophethood)
المعاد (afterlife)
الأمر والنهي (command and prohibition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three types of contents in revealed books?
A) Proofs, commands, stories
B) Tawhid, prophethood, afterlife
C) Command, prohibition, reward
D) Stories only

Key Takeaway
The contents of revealed books are inherently opposed to determinism because they presuppose human responsibility.

?

Why Proofs and Commands Are Meaningless Under Jabr
Detailed arguments that jabr undermines proofs and commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific rational arguments that show how the doctrine of jabr renders divine proofs and commands futile.

Core Idea
If jabr is true, both proofs and commands become useless.

Building on the classification, the author now delves into the logical consequences of jabr. He presents a dilemma for proofs and an analogy for commands to demonstrate that determinism strips them of any rational purpose. This lesson explains these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Follow the dilemma about proofs
The author presents two possibilities: if God creates knowledge, proofs are unnecessary; if not, proofs are useless. Both lead to the conclusion that proofs are futile under jabr.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
2
Consider the analogy for commands
The author gives the example of a person bound and thrown from a mountain, then commanded to return. This shows that commands without capacity are absurd.
The analogy makes the abstract point concrete.
3
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Even though the passage ends mid-sentence, the implication is clear: under jabr, all divine obligations become absurd because human agents lack real capacity.

التنوع الأول الاستحلال الإجمالي وتقريره : إن المقصود من جميع كتب الله المنزلة . إما تقرير الدلائل على التوحيد والنبوة والمعاد . وإما الأمر والنهي، والمدح والذم ، والثواب والعقاب وإما القصص لإرادة أن يعتبر المكلف ، فيحترز عن مخالفة تكاليف الله تعالى . فهذه هي المعاقد الكلية ، والمطالب الأصلية من كتب الله المنزلة . وكلها مبطلة للقول بالجبر . أما تقرير الدلائل على التوحيد والنبوة والمعاد وسائر المطالب ، فالكل على مذهب الجبر عبث باطل . لأنه تعالى إن كان قد خلق العلم والمعرفة بتلك الأشياء ، فلا حاجة به البتة الى معرفة الدلائل . وإن كان الله لم يخلق العلم في المكلف ، لم ينتفع المكلف البتة بشيء من تلك الدلائل فعلى كلا التقديرين يكون نصب الدلائل وتقريرها وإيضاحها : عبث عبثاً ضائعاً ، على مذهب الجبر . وأما الأمر والنهي فهو أيضاً ضائع على مذهب الجبر . لأن الأمر والنهي إنما يحسنان إذا كان المأمور والمنهي قادراً على الفعل متمكناً منه . ألا ترى أن من قيد يداه ورجلاه ورمي من شاهق جبل ، ثم أمر بالرجوع، أو نهي عن النزول : كان ذلك سفهاً وعبثاً . ولو كان كل فعل : خلقه الله تعالى ، لكان جميع تكاليف الله تعالى كذلك . لأن الذي خلقه
The first type: general consideration of rendering [divine commands] void, and its explanation: The purpose of all revealed books of God is either the establishment of proofs for tawhid (divine unity), prophethood, and the afterlife, or command and prohibition, praise and blame, reward and punishment, or the stories, so that the accountable person may take heed and guard against violating the obligations of God Most High. So these are the universal bonds and the original demands from the revealed books of God. And all of them nullify the doctrine of jabr (compulsion/determinism). As for the establishment of proofs for tawhid, prophethood, the afterlife, and the remaining demands, all of it, according to the madhhab (school) of jabr, is vain and false. Because if He Most High has created knowledge and cognition of those things, then there is absolutely no need for knowledge of the proofs. And if God did not create knowledge in the accountable person, the accountable person would benefit absolutely nothing from any of those proofs. So on both suppositions, the setting up of proofs, their establishment, and their clarification is futile, utterly wasted, according to the madhhab of jabr. And as for command and prohibition, it is also wasted according to the madhhab of jabr. Because command and prohibition are only good if the one commanded and forbidden is capable of and able to perform the action. Do you not see that one whose hands and feet are tied and who is thrown from a high mountain, then is commanded to return or forbidden from descending? That would be foolishness and absurdity. And if every action were created by God Most High, then all obligations of God Most High would be like that. Because the one who created it...
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the doctrine of jabr (determinism) based on the contents of revealed books. The author first categorizes the purposes of all revealed books into three types: (1) establishing proofs for tawhid, prophethood, and the afterlife; (2) commands and prohibitions along with praise, blame, reward, and punishment; and (3) stories intended for moral reflection. These are described as the universal bonds and original demands of revelation. The author then asserts that all of these are invalidated by the doctrine of jabr. For the first type, the author presents a dilemma: if God has already created knowledge of these matters, then the proofs are unnecessary; if God does not create knowledge, then the proofs are of no benefit. Either way, setting up proofs is futile. For the second type, commands and prohibitions, the author argues that they are only meaningful if the subject is capable of performing or refraining from the action. An analogy is given: a person bound and thrown from a mountain cannot be commanded to return or forbidden from descending without absurdity. If all actions are directly created by God, then human obligation becomes similarly absurd. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the implied conclusion is that jabr leads to the invalidation of all divine commands and revelation.

Key Terms
الجبر (jabr)
الدلائل (proofs)
الأمر والنهي (command and prohibition)
القدرة (capacity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies that God has any role in human actions.
Correction: The author only rejects the jabr view that eliminates human capacity, not divine omnipotence.

Checkpoint

C) A king commanding a servant
D) A parent commanding a child

Key Takeaway
Under jabr, divine proofs become unnecessary or ineffective, and divine commands become absurd because they require human capacity.

Refutation of Jabr Through the Purposes of Revelation
The author refutes Jabr by showing it nullifies the four purposes of revealed scriptures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the doctrine of jabr (compulsion) nullifies the four main purposes of revealed scriptures: proofs, commands, praise/blame, and stories.

Core Idea
Jabr makes divine revelation pointless by stripping meaning from commands, proofs, and moral accountability.

The author argues that the doctrine of jabr (compulsion) is incompatible with the purposes of revelation. If all actions are created by God, then commands, prohibitions, and even the stories meant to teach lessons become absurd. This lesson examines the four key purposes of scripture and shows how each is rendered futile under jabr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's structure: he lists four purposes and then refutes each.
The passage uses a strong analogy involving a tied person thrown from a mountain to illustrate the absurdity of commands under jabr.
2
Identify the four purposes of revelation
The author lists four primary objectives of all revealed books. Find each one in the text and write them down.
They are: establishing proofs, commands/prohibitions, praise/blame/reward/punishment, and stories for reflection.
3
Understand the refutation of each purpose
For each purpose, the author explains why it becomes futile under jabr. Pay special attention to the analogy for commands and prohibitions.
The analogy demonstrates that commands are meaningless if the person has no real ability to act.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author uses common moral intuition to show that jabr leads to absurd consequences. Do you find the argument convincing?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنوع الأول الاستحلال الإجمالي وتقريره : إن المقصود من جميع كتب الله المنزلة . إما تقرير الدلائل على التوحيد والنبوة والمعاد . وإما الأمر والنهي، والمدح والذم ، والثواب والعقاب وإما القصص لإرادة أن المطالب ، فالكل على مذهب الجبر عبث باطل . لأنه تعالى إن كان قد خلق العلم والمعرفة بتلك الأشياء ، فلا حاجة به البتة الى معرفة الدلائل . وإن كان الله لم يخلق العلم في المكلف ، لم ينتفع المكلف البتة بشيء من تلك الدلائل فعلى كلا التقديرين [ يكون ] نصب الدلائل وتقريرها وإيضاحها : عبث عبثاً ضائعاً ، على مذهب الجبر . وأما الأمر والنهي فهو أيضاً ضائع على مذهب الجبر . لأن الأمر والنهي إنما يحسنان إذا كان المأمور والمنهي قادراً على الفعل متمكناً منه . ألا ترى أن من قيد يداه ورجلاه ورمي من شاهق جبل ، ثم أمر بالرجوع، أو نهي عن النزول : كان ذلك سفهاً وعبثاً . ولو كان [ كل ] فعل : خلقه الله تعالى ، لكان جميع تكاليف الله تعالى كذلك . لأن الذي خلقه الله تعالى فيه كان واجب الحصول . فكان الأمر به جارياً مجرى ما إذا يقيد الإنسان يدي عبده ورجليه ، ويرميه من شاهق الجبل ، ثم يقول [ له ] : انزل ، وإن [ لم ] تنزل عذبتك . والذي ما خلقه الله تعالى فيه [ يكون ] ممتنع الحصول، فكان الأمر به جاريا مجري ما إذا يقيد يديه ورجليه ويرميه من شاهق الجبل ، ثم يقول له : ارجع وان لم ترجع عذبتك . ومعلوم : أن أمثال هذه التكاليف عبث فاسد . فثبت : أن القول بالجبر يفضي إلى أن تكون جميع تكاليف الله باطلة ضائعة . وأما المدح والذم والوعد والوعيد فكل ذلك إنما يحسن إذا كان المخاطب قادراً على الفعل والترك ، متمكناً منهما . حتى أنه إذا أطاع باختياره مدح وأثيب ، وإن عصى باختياره ذم وعوقب فأما إذا خلق الكفر فيه ، شاء أم أب ، ثم يعاقبه عليه أبد الآباد ، ويذمه عليه ، ويقول : لم فعلت ؟ فهذا محض الكذب ، ومحض الظلم والسفه . وأما القصص المذكورة في كتب الله المنزلة . فالفائدة منها : أن يعتبر المكلف بها ، فيقدم على الطاعة ، وينزجر عن المعصية . كما قال تعالى : ( لقد كان في قصصهم عبرة لأولي الألباب ) وهذه الفائدة إنما تحصل إذا كان العبد قادراً على الاختيار أولاً ، وقادراً على الطاعة والمعصية ثانياً . فأما إذا كان لا قدرة له على الطاعة ولا على المعصية البتة ، كان ذكر الاعتبار عبثاً محضاً . لأن الله تعالى إن خلق الطاعة فيه ، فهي حاصلة . سواء حصل ذلك الاعتبار أو لم يحصل ، وإن لم يخلقها امتنع حصولها . سواء حصل هناك اعتبار ، أو لم يحصل . فكان ذكر القصص الإرادة
The first type: general consideration of what is permitted (istihlāl ijmalī) and its explanation: The intended purpose of all the revealed books of God is either: establishing proofs for tawhid (monotheism), prophethood, and the resurrection; or commands and prohibitions, praise and blame, reward and punishment; or stories (al-qaṣaṣ) so that the responsible person (mukallaf) may take heed and guard against opposing the obligations (takālīf) of God Almighty. These are the universal principles and the primary objectives of the revealed books of God. And all of them invalidate the doctrine of jabr (compulsion). As for establishing the proofs for tawhid, prophethood, resurrection, and other objectives, everything according to the doctrine of jabr is futile and false. Because if God Almighty has created knowledge and cognition of those things, then there is no need at all to know the proofs. And if God did not create knowledge in the responsible person, the responsible person would not benefit at all from any of those proofs. So in both cases, the establishment of proofs, their clarification, and their explanation are wasted and futile according to the doctrine of jabr. As for commands and prohibitions, they are also wasted according to the doctrine of jabr. Because commands and prohibitions are only good when the one commanded and forbidden is able to act and has the power to do so. Do you not see that if a person's hands and feet are tied and he is thrown from a high mountain, then he is commanded to return or forbidden from descending, that would be foolishness and futility? And if every act is created by God Almighty, then all the obligations (takālīf) of God Almighty would be similar. Because that which God Almighty has created in him is necessarily occurring. So commanding it is like a situation where a person ties the hands and feet of his servant, throws him from a high mountain, then says to him: 'Descend, and if you do not descend I will punish you.' And that which God Almighty has not created in him is impossible to occur, so commanding it is like a situation where he ties his hands and feet and throws him from a high mountain, then says to him: 'Return, and if you do not return I will punish you.' And it is known that such obligations are corrupt futility. So it is established that the doctrine of jabr leads to all the obligations of God being false and wasted. As for praise and blame, promise and threat, all that is only good when the addressed person is able to do and refrain, having power over both. Such that if he obeys by his choice he is praised and rewarded, and if he disobeys by his choice he is blamed and punished. But if [God] creates disbelief in him, whether he wills or not, then punishes him for it forever and ever, and blames him for it, and says: 'Why did you do that?' This is pure lying, pure injustice and foolishness. As for the stories mentioned in the revealed books of God, the benefit from them is that the responsible person may take heed through them, so that he proceeds to obedience and refrains from disobedience, as God Almighty said: 'Indeed in their stories is a lesson for those of understanding.' And this benefit only occurs if the servant is able to choose first, and able to obey and disobey second. But if he has no ability at all for obedience or disobedience, then mentioning the lesson is pure futility. Because if God Almighty creates obedience in him, it occurs whether that lesson occurs or not; and if He does not create it, its occurrence is impossible whether there is a lesson or not. So mentioning the stories [is] intention? (al-irāda?)
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of jabr (compulsion) by demonstrating that it renders the entire purpose of divine revelation meaningless. The author begins by categorizing the four main objectives of all revealed books: (1) establishing proofs for tawhid, prophethood, and the resurrection; (2) commands and prohibitions along with praise, blame, reward, and punishment; (3) stories meant to inspire reflection and fear of God; and (4) the general aim of guiding the mukallaf (responsible person) to avoid violating God's obligations. He then argues that each of these becomes absurd if jabr is true.

First, regarding proofs: if God has already created knowledge of these matters in the person, proofs are unnecessary; if He has not created it, proofs cannot benefit. Hence, establishing proofs is futile.

Second, regarding commands and prohibitions: these are only meaningful if the person has genuine ability to act or refrain. The author uses a powerful analogy: if someone's hands and feet are tied and he is thrown from a mountain, commanding him to return or forbidding him from descending is clearly foolish. Similarly, if every act is created by God, then what God has created in a person is inevitable, so commanding it is pointless (like commanding the chained man to descend) and what God has not created is impossible, so commanding it is like telling the chained man to return. Both are absurd.

Third, regarding praise and blame, reward and punishment: these are only fitting when the person acts by his own choice. If God creates disbelief in someone and then punishes him eternally, that is injustice and foolishness.

Fourth, regarding stories: their purpose is to provide a lesson (iʿtibār) that motivates obedience and deters sin. This benefit depends on the person having the ability to choose and act. If he has no power at all, the lesson is useless because whether God creates obedience or not, it either happens inevitably or is impossible regardless of the story.

The author concludes that jabr makes all divine obligations vain and empty. The argument is thorough and relies on common moral intuition.

Key Terms
جبر
تكاليف
أمر ونهي
مدح وذم
ثواب وعقاب
قصص
اعتبار
مكلف
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jabr only makes punishment unjust, but commands and proofs still have value.
Correction: The author shows that jabr nullifies all four purposes equally: proofs become either unnecessary or useless, commands become absurd (like commanding a chained person), praise/blame become unjust, and stories lose their instructive value.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are commands and prohibitions (al-amr wa al-nahy) futile under the doctrine of jabr?
A) Because God does not command anything impossible.
B) Because the person either necessarily performs the act (if God created it) or cannot perform it (if God did not), so commanding it is like commanding a tied and thrown person.
C) Because the person has free will and can choose to obey or disobey.
D) Because commands and prohibitions are only for angels, not humans.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of jabr is self-defeating because it strips divine revelation of its very purpose: commands become meaningless, proofs become useless, and moral accountability becomes unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says commands are only meaningful if you have genuine ability. In what area of your life do you act as if you are powerless, ignoring that God's command is a call to choose? What step will you take this week to exercise that choice?
?
If the Qur'an's stories are meant to make you reflect and change, but you read them without feeling personally challenged, are you treating them as the author says jabr would—as useless? Which story will you reread tonight with the intention to act differently?

Divine Decree

First Proof from 'A'udhu Billah' against Jabr
First proof from 'A'udhu billah' that the servant is the agent of his own actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur’anic phrase 'A'udhu billah' refutes the doctrine of Jabr by demonstrating that the servant is the agent of his own actions.

Core Idea
Saying 'A'udhu' proves you are the doer—if Allah did it, you couldn't say it.

This passage presents the first argument from the phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytani al-rajim' against the fatalist doctrine of Jabr. By analyzing the simple act of seeking refuge, the author shows that the servant must be the true agent of his voluntary actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice how the argument is built step by step.
The Arabic contains the key term 'تحصيل الحاصل' which is central to the proof.
2
Identify the core argument
The author argues that the word 'أعوذ' is an acknowledgment of human agency. Find the sentence that explicitly states this.
Look for the phrase 'اعتراف بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه'.
3
Understand the logical principle
The principle of 'تحصيل الحاصل' (actualizing the already actual) is used to show impossibility if Allah creates the action. Grasp why this proves human agency.
This is a key logical tool in kalam arguments about divine power and human action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من وجه تمسكهم بالقرآن أن قالوا: القرآن من الباء من (بسم الله) إلى السين من قوله: (من الجنة والناس) ناطق بفساد مذهب الجبر. ونحن نشرح ذلك في سورة الفاتحة ليقاس عليه الباقي. ورتبناه على فصول: الفصل الأول في [المبلث؟] المستنبطة من قولنا: (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم). قالوا: هذا يدل على بطلان القول بالجبر من وجوه: الأول: إن قولنا: (أعوذ) اعتراف يكون العبد فاعلاً. ولو كان فعل العبد مخلوقاً لله تعالى، لامتنع كونه فاعلاً للاستعاذة. لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، لأن الله تعالى إذا خلق ذلك الفعل، لم يقدر العبد على الامتناع منه، وإن لم يخلقه الله تعالى فيه، استحال منه الإقدام على تحصيله. فثبت: أن قوله: (أعوذ) اعتراف بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه.
The second type regarding their clinging to the Quran: they said: The Quran from the letter ba in 'Bismi Allah' to the letter sin in His saying: 'min al-jinnati wa al-nas' is expressive of the corruption of the doctrine of Jabr (fatalism/compulsion). And we will explain that in Surat al-Fatiha so that the rest can be analogized to it. And we arranged it into chapters: The first chapter regarding the [؟] derived from our saying: 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytani al-rajim' (I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed Satan). They said: This indicates the invalidity of the claim of Jabr from several aspects: The first: that our saying: 'A'udhu' (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that the servant is a doer (fa'il). And if the action of the servant were created by Allah the Exalted, it would be impossible for him to be a doer of seeking refuge. Because the actualizing of what is already actual (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible; because if Allah the Exalted created that action, the servant would not be able to refrain from it, and if Allah the Exalted did not create it in him, it would be impossible for him to proceed to actualize it. So it is established: that his saying: 'A'udhu' is an acknowledgment of his being a bringer-into-existence (mujid) of his own actions.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Qur'anic phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytani al-rajim' against the doctrine of Jabr (fatalism). The author begins by stating that the entire Qur'an, from the basmala to the end of Surat al-Nas, refutes Jabr. He then focuses on Surat al-Fatiha and specifically the opening phrase 'A'udhu billah'. The first chapter deals with the derived meaning from this phrase. The first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) argues as follows: The word 'A'udhu' (I seek refuge) is an action that the servant performs voluntarily; by saying it, the servant implicitly acknowledges that he is a doer (fa'il) of that action. If the servant's action were created entirely by Allah, then the servant could not properly be called a 'doer' of that action—specifically, the act of seeking refuge. This is because actualizing something that already exists (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible. If Allah creates the action, the servant cannot refrain from it, so he has no choice. If Allah does not create it, the servant cannot bring it about. Therefore, the very utterance of 'A'udhu' demonstrates that the servant is the one who brings his own actions into existence (mujid li-af'al nafsihi), thereby refuting the Jabrite claim that all actions are directly created by Allah without any human agency.

Key Terms
جبر
أعوذ
فاعل
تحصيل الحاصل
موجد لأفعال نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'A'udhu' is just a meaningless formula; it doesn't prove human agency.
Correction: The passage argues that uttering 'A'udhu' is an act of seeking refuge that logically implies the servant is the doer of that act, thus proving agency.

Checkpoint
What does the word 'أعوذ' (I seek refuge) imply according to the first proof in this passage?
A) That Allah alone is the creator of all actions, including the act of seeking refuge.
B) That the servant is the doer of the action of seeking refuge, thus proving his agency.
C) That the action is jointly created by both Allah and the servant.
D) That seeking refuge is an impossible action.

Key Takeaway
The simple utterance of 'A'udhu billah' contains a profound theological argument: because you say it, you prove you are the agent of your own actions, refuting the doctrine of Jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'A'udhu billah', do you truly experience yourself as the one actively seeking refuge, or do you say it as a passive ritual? How does your answer affect the sincerity of your seeking?
?
If you believe that Allah creates every action, including your sins, how do you cope with feelings of guilt? Does this passage challenge you to accept personal responsibility for your wrongdoings?
?
Reflect on a moment you sinned and felt regret. Does this proof from 'A'udhu' convince you that you had the power to choose otherwise? If so, what will you change in your next moment of temptation?

Introduction and First Argument: The Act of Seeking Refuge Proves Human Agency
First argument from the phrase 'A'udhu' against Jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the simple act of saying 'A'udhu billah' rationally establishes that the servant is the doer of his own actions, refuting the doctrine of Jabr.

Core Idea
Saying 'I seek refuge' is an admission that you are the one doing the seeking.

The author begins by asserting that the Qur'an from its very first verse refutes Jabr. He uses the common Muslim phrase 'A'udhu billah' as a test case. In this lesson, we examine the first of six arguments: the linguistic and logical implications of the word 'A'udhu'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how the author sets up the framework for proving Jabr false.
The passage includes the introductory statement about the Qur'an's refutation and then presents the first argument.
2
Understand the logical dilemma
The argument presents a dichotomy: either Allah creates the act of seeking refuge or the servant does. Consider the two possibilities: if Allah creates it, the servant cannot avoid it; if not, the servant cannot originate it. Both lead to the impossibility of the act.
This is a classic kalam reductio ad absurdum.
3
See the conclusion
The argument concludes that the very utterance of 'A'udhu' is an acknowledgment that the servant is the originator of his own acts. Reflect on why this refutes Jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من وجهه تمسكهم بالقرآن أن قالوا: القرآن من الباء من (بسم الله) إلى السين من قوله: «من الجنة والناس» ناطق بفساد مذهب الجبر. ونحن نشرح ذلك في سورة الفاتحة. ليقاس عليه الباقي. ورتبناه على فصول: الفصل الأول في المباحث المستنبطة من قولنا: (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم). قالوا: هذا يدل على بطلان القول بالجبر من وجوه:
الأول: إن قولنا: (أعوذ) اعتراف يكون العبد فاعلاً. ولو كان فعل العبد مخلوقاً لله تعالى، لامتنع كونه فاعلاً للاستعاذة. لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، لأن الله تعالى إذا خلق ذلك الفعل، لم يقدر العبد على الامتناع منه، وإن لم يخلقه الله تعالى فيه، استحال منه الإقدام على تحصيله. فثبت: أن قوله: (أعوذ) اعتراف بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه.
الثاني: إن الاستعاذة بالله، إنما تعقل، لو لم يكن الله هو الفاعل للأمور التي منها يستعاذ. أما إذا كان الفاعل لها هو الله تعالى، امتنع أن يستعاذ به تعالى من تلك الأفعال. لأنه يصير كأنه استعاذ بالله، من الله، في الفعل الذي فعله الله تعالى.
الثالث: إن إقدام العبد على الاستعاذة من المعاصي، يدل على أن العبد غير راضي بها. ولو كانت المعاصي مخلوقة لله تعالى، لزم أن لا يكون العبد راضياً بقضاء الله. وذلك كفر.
الرابع: إن الاستعاذة بالله من الشيطان، إنما تعقل لو كانت الوسوسة فعلاً للشيطان. أما إذا كانت فعلاً لله تعالى، ولم يكن للشيطان فيها أثر، فكيف يعقل أن يستعاذ من شر الشيطان؟
الخامس: إن لفظ الشيطان، مشتق. إما من شطن، وهو البعد. أو من شاط، وهو الاحتراف. وعلى التقديرين فهو اسم ذم. والذم إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل. فإذا كان الشيطان لا قدرة له على الفعل لم يحسن ذمه.
السادس: إن (الرجيم) معناه المرجوم. وهو إشارة إلى كونه معذباً. وذلك إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل. لا في حق العاجز عنه.
فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن قوله (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم) يبطل القول بالجبر. ولا يقال: إنا لا نقول بالجبر، بل نقول: العبد مكتسب. لأنا نقول: هذا ليس بشيء. فإن العبد إما أن يمكنه تحصيل شيء وتكوينه، أو لا يمكنه ذلك. فإن أمكنه فهو مذهبنا. وإن كان لا يمكنه فقد توجهت السؤالات المذكورة. وليس بين النفي والإثبات واسطة. فثبت: أن إلغاء لفظ الكسب في الشيء: محض التزوير.
قال أهل السنة والجماعة: إن كانت الوجوه التي استنبطوها من قولنا:
The second type of their adherence to the Qur'an: they said: The Qur'an from the ba' of (Bismillah) to the sin of His saying: 'min al-jinnati wa al-nas' speaks of the corruption of the doctrine of Jabr. And we explain that in Surat al-Fatihah, for the rest to be measured by it. And we arranged it into chapters: The first chapter on the investigations derived from our saying: (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). They said: This indicates the falsity of the statement of Jabr from aspects:
The first: That our saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that the servant is an agent. And if the act of the servant were created by Allah Almighty, it would be impossible for him to be an agent of seeking refuge. Because obtaining what has already been obtained is impossible, for if Allah Almighty created that act, the servant would not be able to refrain from it, and if Allah did not create it in him, it would be impossible for him to proceed to obtain it. So it is established: that his saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that he is the originator of his own acts.
The second: That seeking refuge in Allah is only conceivable if Allah is not the doer of the things from which refuge is sought. But if the doer of them is Allah Almighty, it is impossible to seek refuge in Him from those acts. Because it becomes as if he sought refuge in Allah from Allah, in the act that Allah Almighty did.
The third: That the servant's proceeding to seek refuge from sins indicates that the servant is not pleased with them. And if sins were created by Allah Almighty, it would necessarily follow that the servant is not pleased with Allah's decree. And that is disbelief.
The fourth: That seeking refuge in Allah from Satan is only conceivable if whispering is an act of Satan. But if it is an act of Allah Almighty, and Satan has no effect in it, how can it be conceived to seek refuge from the evil of Satan?
The fifth: That the word 'Satan' is derived. Either from 'shatana' which means distance, or from 'shata' which means 'al-ihtiraf' (deviation/burning). In both cases it is a noun of blame. And blame is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act. So if Satan has no power over the act, his blame is not appropriate.
The sixth: That 'al-rajim' means the one who is stoned. And it is an indication of his being punished. And that is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act, not regarding one who is incapable of it.
So it is established by what we have mentioned: that his saying (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan) invalidates the statement of Jabr. And let it not be said: We do not say Jabr, rather we say the servant is an acquirer (muktasib). For we say: This is nothing. For the servant either can obtain and originate something, or he cannot. If he can, then it is our doctrine. And if he cannot, then the aforementioned questions are directed. And there is no intermediate between negation and affirmation. So it is established: that canceling the word 'kasb' in the matter is pure forgery.
Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: If the aspects that they derived from our saying:
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument refuting the doctrine of Jabr (absolute determinism) by drawing six rational and linguistic proofs from the Qur'anic phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytani al-rajim' (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). The author first explains that the Qur'an from its very beginning refutes Jabr, and he focuses on this specific phrase, which will serve as a paradigm for other passages. The six arguments proceed as follows:

1. The act of saying 'I seek refuge' is an acknowledgment that the servant is the doer of his own action. If Allah had created that act, the servant could not be its agent, because either Allah creates it and the servant cannot avoid it (so he has no choice), or Allah does not create it and the servant cannot originate it (so he cannot act). Thus, the phrase proves human agency.

2. Seeking refuge in Allah from something only makes sense if Allah is not the doer of that thing. If Allah were the doer of the evil from which refuge is sought, the act of seeking refuge would become a request for protection from Allah's own act—an absurdity.

3. The servant's seeking refuge from sins shows he is displeased with them. If sins were created by Allah, then being displeased with them would imply being displeased with Allah's decree, which is tantamount to disbelief (kufr).

4. Seeking refuge from Satan's evil only makes sense if Satan himself causes that evil (e.g., whispering). If Allah were the sole creator of that evil, Satan would have no part in it, and seeking refuge from him would be pointless.

5. The term 'shaytan' is derived from roots implying distance or deviation (or burning), and in either case it is a term of blame. Blame is only appropriate for a being with genuine power to act. If Satan has no power, blaming him would be unjust.

6. Similarly, 'al-rajim' (the accursed/stoned) indicates punishment, which is only appropriate for a being capable of action. If Satan is passive, such a description would be meaningless.

After these six proofs, the author anticipates an objection from the Jabriyya who might claim they do not hold pure Jabr but rather the theory of kasb (acquisition). The author dismisses this as empty: either the servant truly has the ability to originate his actions (in which case it is the Ash'ari position?) or he does not (in which case the same criticisms apply). There is no middle ground, and invoking 'kasb' without genuine power is mere rhetoric.

The passage concludes with a statement from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, though the text cuts off mid-sentence. The overall argument is that the very phrase used by Muslims in daily worship presupposes human agency, thereby refuting extreme determinism.

Key Terms
أعوذ
استعاذة
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant has no part in his actions; Allah creates all acts.
Correction: The act of saying 'A'udhu' proves that the servant is the originator of his own actions, because if Allah were the sole creator, the act would be either compelled or impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what does the utterance of 'A'udhu' acknowledge?
A) That Allah is the creator of all acts.
B) That the servant is the originator of his own acts.
C) That the servant is compelled to act.
D) That the act of seeking refuge is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The very act of seeking refuge in Allah is a rational proof that humans are agents of their own actions, contradicting absolute determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'A'udhu billah,' do you truly recognize yourself as the one choosing to seek refuge, or do you recite it mechanically? How does this lesson demand you to be more conscious of your agency in that moment?
?
If your own words can serve as proof of your beliefs, then what does your daily recitation of 'A'udhu' reveal about your understanding of human responsibility? Are you willing to accept that you are accountable for your choices, even as you rely on Allah?
?
This argument exposes the impossibility of pure determinism. Is there an area of your life where you have been acting as if you have no choice, blaming circumstances or fate? How will you reclaim your agency in that area starting today?

Second Argument: The Incoherence of Seeking Refuge from Allah's Own Acts
Second argument from the phrase 'A'udhu' against Jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why seeking refuge in Allah from something that Allah Himself is said to do is logically absurd, thereby refuting Jabr.

Core Idea
You cannot seek refuge in Allah from Allah's own acts.

The second argument highlights a logical contradiction in the Jabri view: if Allah is the sole doer of all actions, including evil, then seeking refuge in Allah from that evil becomes an absurd request for protection from Allah Himself. This argument exposes the incoherence of the determinist position.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and English versions of the second argument. Note the key point: seeking refuge only makes sense if the one sought refuge from is not Allah.
2
Identify the absurdity
If Allah is the doer of the evil, then seeking refuge from it in Allah is like saying 'I seek refuge in You from You' in that specific act.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Apply to Jabr
Recognize that this argument presupposes that the evil from which we seek refuge is genuinely evil and not caused by Allah. If it were caused by Allah, the very act of seeking refuge would be illogical.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is the doer of all actions, including evil, so seeking refuge from evil is still valid because one seeks refuge from the evil itself.
Correction: The argument shows that if Allah is the doer, seeking refuge from that act in Allah is like seeking refuge from Allah in Allah, which is absurd. The act of isti'adha presupposes a source of evil distinct from Allah.

Checkpoint
Why is seeking refuge in Allah from something impossible if Allah is the doer of that thing?
A) Because Allah cannot protect from what He wills.
B) Because it becomes seeking refuge from Allah in Allah, which is absurd.
C) Because the servant should seek refuge from something else.
D) Because seeking refuge is only valid against Satan.

Key Takeaway
Seeking refuge in Allah from evil is only meaningful if the evil is not directly caused by Allah, refuting the idea that all acts are from Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'A'udhu billahi min ash-shaytan ar-rajim', do you truly believe that the whispering you flee is not from Allah? Or have you ever, in a moment of despair, blamed Allah for your own evil inclinations? Let this argument hold your tongue and your heart accountable today.
?
If every evil thought or action came directly from Allah, your plea for refuge would be a request for Allah to undo His own work. Does your du'a reflect that contradiction? Or do you live as if He is not the author of evil, trusting that He only permits what He hates? Let this truth reshape how you cry to Him.
?
Consider a recent trial you faced. Did you seek refuge in Allah from its harm while secretly resenting Him for sending it? This lesson calls you to examine: do you treat Allah as both the refuge and the source of evil? Repent and run to Him, for He is your refuge from what He wills but does not cause.

Third Argument: Displeasure with Sin Implies Displeasure with Divine Decree
Third argument from the phrase 'A'udhu' against Jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if sins were directly created by Allah, then seeking refuge from them would imply dissatisfaction with Allah's decree, which is tantamount to disbelief.

Core Idea
Being displeased with sin is only valid if sin is not from Allah.

The third argument draws a theological implication: when a Muslim seeks refuge from sins, he expresses displeasure with those sins. If sins were directly created by Allah, such displeasure would mean being displeased with Allah's own decree, which is kufr. Thus, the practice of seeking refuge from sin confirms that sins are not created by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the connection between seeking refuge from sin and being displeased with sin.
2
Trace the implication
If sin were created by Allah, being displeased with sin would be displeased with Allah's decree. The text declares this to be kufr.
3
Conclude against Jabr
Since Muslims do seek refuge from sin and are not guilty of kufr, sin cannot be directly created by Allah. Therefore, the servant must be responsible for his sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من وجهه تمسكهم بالقرآن أن قالوا: القرآن من الباء من (بسم الله) إلى السين من قوله: «من الجنة والناس» ناطق بفساد مذهب الجبر. ونحن نشرح ذلك في سورة الفاتحة. ليقاس عليه الباقي. ورتبناه على فصول: الفصل الأول في المباحث المستنبطة من قولنا: (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم). قالوا: هذا يدل على بطلان القول بالجبر من وجوه:
الأول: إن قولنا: (أعوذ) اعتراف يكون العبد فاعلاً. ولو كان فعل العبد مخلوقاً لله تعالى، لامتنع كونه فاعلاً للاستعاذة. لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، لأن الله تعالى إذا خلق ذلك الفعل، لم يقدر العبد على الامتناع منه، وإن لم يخلقه الله تعالى فيه، استحال منه الإقدام على تحصيله. فثبت: أن قوله: (أعوذ) اعتراف بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه.
الثاني: إن الاستعاذة بالله، إنما تعقل، لو لم يكن الله هو الفاعل للأمور التي منها يستعاذ. أما إذا كان الفاعل لها هو الله تعالى، امتنع أن يستعاذ به تعالى من تلك الأفعال. لأنه يصير كأنه استعاذ بالله، من الله، في الفعل الذي فعله الله تعالى.
الثالث: إن إقدام العبد على الاستعاذة من المعاصي، يدل على أن العبد غير راضي بها. ولو كانت المعاصي مخلوقة لله تعالى، لزم أن لا يكون العبد راضياً بقضاء الله. وذلك كفر.
الرابع: إن الاستعاذة بالله من الشيطان، إنما تعقل لو كانت الوسوسة فعلاً للشيطان. أما إذا كانت فعلاً لله تعالى، ولم يكن للشيطان فيها أثر، فكيف يعقل أن يستعاذ من شر الشيطان؟
الخامس: إن لفظ الشيطان، مشتق. إما من شطن، وهو البعد. أو من شاط، وهو الاحتراف. وعلى التقديرين فهو اسم ذم. والذم إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل. فإذا كان الشيطان لا قدرة له على الفعل لم يحسن ذمه.
السادس: إن (الرجيم) معناه المرجوم. وهو إشارة إلى كونه معذباً. وذلك إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل. لا في حق العاجز عنه.
فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن قوله (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم) يبطل القول بالجبر. ولا يقال: إنا لا نقول بالجبر، بل نقول: العبد مكتسب. لأنا نقول: هذا ليس بشيء. فإن العبد إما أن يمكنه تحصيل شيء وتكوينه، أو لا يمكنه ذلك. فإن أمكنه فهو مذهبنا. وإن كان لا يمكنه فقد توجهت السؤالات المذكورة. وليس بين النفي والإثبات واسطة. فثبت: أن إلغاء لفظ الكسب في الشيء: محض التزوير.
قال أهل السنة والجماعة: إن كانت الوجوه التي استنبطوها من قولنا:
The second type of their adherence to the Qur'an: they said: The Qur'an from the ba' of (Bismillah) to the sin of His saying: 'min al-jinnati wa al-nas' speaks of the corruption of the doctrine of Jabr. And we explain that in Surat al-Fatihah, for the rest to be measured by it. And we arranged it into chapters: The first chapter on the investigations derived from our saying: (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). They said: This indicates the falsity of the statement of Jabr from aspects:
The first: That our saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that the servant is an agent. And if the act of the servant were created by Allah Almighty, it would be impossible for him to be an agent of seeking refuge. Because obtaining what has already been obtained is impossible, for if Allah Almighty created that act, the servant would not be able to refrain from it, and if Allah did not create it in him, it would be impossible for him to proceed to obtain it. So it is established: that his saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that he is the originator of his own acts.
The second: That seeking refuge in Allah is only conceivable if Allah is not the doer of the things from which refuge is sought. But if the doer of them is Allah Almighty, it is impossible to seek refuge in Him from those acts. Because it becomes as if he sought refuge in Allah from Allah, in the act that Allah Almighty did.
The third: That the servant's proceeding to seek refuge from sins indicates that the servant is not pleased with them. And if sins were created by Allah Almighty, it would necessarily follow that the servant is not pleased with Allah's decree. And that is disbelief.
The fourth: That seeking refuge in Allah from Satan is only conceivable if whispering is an act of Satan. But if it is an act of Allah Almighty, and Satan has no effect in it, how can it be conceived to seek refuge from the evil of Satan?
The fifth: That the word 'Satan' is derived. Either from 'shatana' which means distance, or from 'shata' which means 'al-ihtiraf' (deviation/burning). In both cases it is a noun of blame. And blame is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act. So if Satan has no power over the act, his blame is not appropriate.
The sixth: That 'al-rajim' means the one who is stoned. And it is an indication of his being punished. And that is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act, not regarding one who is incapable of it.
So it is established by what we have mentioned: that his saying (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan) invalidates the statement of Jabr. And let it not be said: We do not say Jabr, rather we say the servant is an acquirer (muktasib). For we say: This is nothing. For the servant either can obtain and originate something, or he cannot. If he can, then it is our doctrine. And if he cannot, then the aforementioned questions are directed. And there is no intermediate between negation and affirmation. So it is established: that canceling the word 'kasb' in the matter is pure forgery.
Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: If the aspects that they derived from our saying:
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument refuting the doctrine of Jabr (absolute determinism) by drawing six rational and linguistic proofs from the Qur'anic phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytani al-rajim' (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). The author first explains that the Qur'an from its very beginning refutes Jabr, and he focuses on this specific phrase, which will serve as a paradigm for other passages. The six arguments proceed as follows:

1. The act of saying 'I seek refuge' is an acknowledgment that the servant is the doer of his own action. If Allah had created that act, the servant could not be its agent, because either Allah creates it and the servant cannot avoid it (so he has no choice), or Allah does not create it and the servant cannot originate it (so he cannot act). Thus, the phrase proves human agency.

2. Seeking refuge in Allah from something only makes sense if Allah is not the doer of that thing. If Allah were the doer of the evil from which refuge is sought, the act of seeking refuge would become a request for protection from Allah's own act—an absurdity.

3. The servant's seeking refuge from sins shows he is displeased with them. If sins were created by Allah, then being displeased with them would imply being displeased with Allah's decree, which is tantamount to disbelief (kufr).

4. Seeking refuge from Satan's evil only makes sense if Satan himself causes that evil (e.g., whispering). If Allah were the sole creator of that evil, Satan would have no part in it, and seeking refuge from him would be pointless.

5. The term 'shaytan' is derived from roots implying distance or deviation (or burning), and in either case it is a term of blame. Blame is only appropriate for a being with genuine power to act. If Satan has no power, blaming him would be unjust.

6. Similarly, 'al-rajim' (the accursed/stoned) indicates punishment, which is only appropriate for a being capable of action. If Satan is passive, such a description would be meaningless.

After these six proofs, the author anticipates an objection from the Jabriyya who might claim they do not hold pure Jabr but rather the theory of kasb (acquisition). The author dismisses this as empty: either the servant truly has the ability to originate his actions (in which case it is the Ash'ari position?) or he does not (in which case the same criticisms apply). There is no middle ground, and invoking 'kasb' without genuine power is mere rhetoric.

The passage concludes with a statement from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, though the text cuts off mid-sentence. The overall argument is that the very phrase used by Muslims in daily worship presupposes human agency, thereby refuting extreme determinism.

Key Terms
استعاذة
معصية
قضاء
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sins are created by Allah, and believers can still dislike them without it being a problem.
Correction: The argument shows that disliking what Allah has decreed is kufr. Therefore, since believers dislike sins, sins cannot be decreed by Allah.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument claim would be the consequence if sins were created by Allah?
A) The servant would be forced to sin.
B) The servant would not be pleased with Allah's decree, which is disbelief.
C) The servant would be sinless.
D) The servant would not need to seek refuge.

Key Takeaway
Seeking refuge from sin implies displeasure with sin, which would be kufr if sin were from Allah, so sin must not be from Allah directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself resenting a difficult situation that resulted from your own sin, blaming Allah instead of yourself? How does this argument force you to take responsibility for your choices?
?
When you say 'A'udhu billah' before reciting Quran, do you genuinely feel displeasure with the sins you seek refuge from, or has it become a mechanical phrase? If your heart is not displeased, what does that reveal about your understanding of where sin comes from?
?
This argument says that if sin were from Allah, being displeased with sin would be kufr. Are you willing to fully accept that your sins are your own doing, and that being displeased with your sin is right only because it is not from Allah? What must you change in your attitude toward your own failures?

Fourth Argument: Seeking Refuge from Satan's Whispering
Fourth argument from the phrase 'A'udhu' against Jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeking refuge from Satan only makes sense if Satan genuinely causes evil (like whispering), refuting the idea that Allah creates all acts directly.

Core Idea
You can only seek refuge from Satan if Satan actually does something.

The fourth argument focuses on the object of seeking refuge: Satan. When we say 'A'udhu billah min al-shaytan', we assume that Satan is the source of the evil we seek protection from. If Allah were the sole creator of all acts, including Satan's whispering, then Satan would be innocent, and seeking refuge from him would be nonsensical.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify what the act of seeking refuge from Satan presupposes.
2
Analyze the condition
The argument states that seeking refuge from Satan is only meaningful if whispering is Satan's act. Consider what happens if whispering is instead created by Allah.
3
See the absurdity
If Allah creates the whispering and Satan has no role, seeking refuge from Satan is pointless. This absurdity refutes Jabr.

According to the fourth argument, under what condition is seeking refuge from Satan conceivable?
A) If Satan is created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

This argument confronts you: your own practice assumes Satan's agency. Will you accept the implication and live accordingly, or will you ignore the contradiction in your actions?
?

Perhaps you forgot that Satan is real and active. Make a firm intention to say isti'adha the next time a bad thought comes, and reflect on how that changes the struggle.

Fifth Argument: The Term 'Shaytan' Implies Blameworthiness
Fifth argument from the phrase 'A'udhu' against Jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the linguistic derivation of the word 'shaytan' indicates it is a term of blame, which is only appropriate if Satan has genuine power to act.

Core Idea
Calling someone 'shaytan' blames them, so they must have power to act.

The fifth argument turns to linguistic analysis. The word 'shaytan' (Satan) is derived from roots that imply distance or deviation, and in either case it is a term of blame (ism dham). Blame is only meaningful if the one blamed is capable of performing the blameworthy act. If Satan were just a passive instrument, blaming him would be unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two possible derivations of 'shaytan'.
2
Understand 'ism dham'
The text says 'shaytan' is a noun of blame. Consider what it means to blame someone.
3
Connect to power
Blame is only appropriate if the blamed has the ability to do otherwise. If Satan has no power (as Jabr claims), blaming him is incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من وجهه تمسكهم بالقرآن أن قالوا: القرآن من الباء من (بسم الله) إلى السين من قوله: «من الجنة والناس» ناطق بفساد مذهب الجبر. ونحن نشرح ذلك في سورة الفاتحة. ليقاس عليه الباقي. ورتبناه على فصول: الفصل الأول في المباحث المستنبطة من قولنا: (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم). قالوا: هذا يدل على بطلان القول بالجبر من وجوه:
الأول: إن قولنا: (أعوذ) اعتراف يكون العبد فاعلاً. ولو كان فعل العبد مخلوقاً لله تعالى، لامتنع كونه فاعلاً للاستعاذة. لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، لأن الله تعالى إذا خلق ذلك الفعل، لم يقدر العبد على الامتناع منه، وإن لم يخلقه الله تعالى فيه، استحال منه الإقدام على تحصيله. فثبت: أن قوله: (أعوذ) اعتراف بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه.
الثاني: إن الاستعاذة بالله، إنما تعقل، لو لم يكن الله هو الفاعل للأمور التي منها يستعاذ. أما إذا كان الفاعل لها هو الله تعالى، امتنع أن يستعاذ به تعالى من تلك الأفعال. لأنه يصير كأنه استعاذ بالله، من الله، في الفعل الذي فعله الله تعالى.
الثالث: إن إقدام العبد على الاستعاذة من المعاصي، يدل على أن العبد غير راضي بها. ولو كانت المعاصي مخلوقة لله تعالى، لزم أن لا يكون العبد راضياً بقضاء الله. وذلك كفر.
الرابع: إن الاستعاذة بالله من الشيطان، إنما تعقل لو كانت الوسوسة فعلاً للشيطان. أما إذا كانت فعلاً لله تعالى، ولم يكن للشيطان فيها أثر، فكيف يعقل أن يستعاذ من شر الشيطان؟
الخامس: إن لفظ الشيطان، مشتق. إما من شطن، وهو البعد. أو من شاط، وهو الاحتراف. وعلى التقديرين فهو اسم ذم. والذم إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل. فإذا كان الشيطان لا قدرة له على الفعل لم يحسن ذمه.
السادس: إن (الرجيم) معناه المرجوم. وهو إشارة إلى كونه معذباً. وذلك إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل. لا في حق العاجز عنه.
فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن قوله (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم) يبطل القول بالجبر. ولا يقال: إنا لا نقول بالجبر، بل نقول: العبد مكتسب. لأنا نقول: هذا ليس بشيء. فإن العبد إما أن يمكنه تحصيل شيء وتكوينه، أو لا يمكنه ذلك. فإن أمكنه فهو مذهبنا. وإن كان لا يمكنه فقد توجهت السؤالات المذكورة. وليس بين النفي والإثبات واسطة. فثبت: أن إلغاء لفظ الكسب في الشيء: محض التزوير.
قال أهل السنة والجماعة: إن كانت الوجوه التي استنبطوها من قولنا:
The second type of their adherence to the Qur'an: they said: The Qur'an from the ba' of (Bismillah) to the sin of His saying: 'min al-jinnati wa al-nas' speaks of the corruption of the doctrine of Jabr. And we explain that in Surat al-Fatihah, for the rest to be measured by it. And we arranged it into chapters: The first chapter on the investigations derived from our saying: (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). They said: This indicates the falsity of the statement of Jabr from aspects:
The first: That our saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that the servant is an agent. And if the act of the servant were created by Allah Almighty, it would be impossible for him to be an agent of seeking refuge. Because obtaining what has already been obtained is impossible, for if Allah Almighty created that act, the servant would not be able to refrain from it, and if Allah did not create it in him, it would be impossible for him to proceed to obtain it. So it is established: that his saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that he is the originator of his own acts.
The second: That seeking refuge in Allah is only conceivable if Allah is not the doer of the things from which refuge is sought. But if the doer of them is Allah Almighty, it is impossible to seek refuge in Him from those acts. Because it becomes as if he sought refuge in Allah from Allah, in the act that Allah Almighty did.
The third: That the servant's proceeding to seek refuge from sins indicates that the servant is not pleased with them. And if sins were created by Allah Almighty, it would necessarily follow that the servant is not pleased with Allah's decree. And that is disbelief.
The fourth: That seeking refuge in Allah from Satan is only conceivable if whispering is an act of Satan. But if it is an act of Allah Almighty, and Satan has no effect in it, how can it be conceived to seek refuge from the evil of Satan?
The fifth: That the word 'Satan' is derived. Either from 'shatana' which means distance, or from 'shata' which means 'al-ihtiraf' (deviation/burning). In both cases it is a noun of blame. And blame is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act. So if Satan has no power over the act, his blame is not appropriate.
The sixth: That 'al-rajim' means the one who is stoned. And it is an indication of his being punished. And that is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act, not regarding one who is incapable of it.
So it is established by what we have mentioned: that his saying (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan) invalidates the statement of Jabr. And let it not be said: We do not say Jabr, rather we say the servant is an acquirer (muktasib). For we say: This is nothing. For the servant either can obtain and originate something, or he cannot. If he can, then it is our doctrine. And if he cannot, then the aforementioned questions are directed. And there is no intermediate between negation and affirmation. So it is established: that canceling the word 'kasb' in the matter is pure forgery.
Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: If the aspects that they derived from our saying:
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument refuting the doctrine of Jabr (absolute determinism) by drawing six rational and linguistic proofs from the Qur'anic phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytani al-rajim' (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). The author first explains that the Qur'an from its very beginning refutes Jabr, and he focuses on this specific phrase, which will serve as a paradigm for other passages. The six arguments proceed as follows:

1. The act of saying 'I seek refuge' is an acknowledgment that the servant is the doer of his own action. If Allah had created that act, the servant could not be its agent, because either Allah creates it and the servant cannot avoid it (so he has no choice), or Allah does not create it and the servant cannot originate it (so he cannot act). Thus, the phrase proves human agency.

2. Seeking refuge in Allah from something only makes sense if Allah is not the doer of that thing. If Allah were the doer of the evil from which refuge is sought, the act of seeking refuge would become a request for protection from Allah's own act—an absurdity.

3. The servant's seeking refuge from sins shows he is displeased with them. If sins were created by Allah, then being displeased with them would imply being displeased with Allah's decree, which is tantamount to disbelief (kufr).

4. Seeking refuge from Satan's evil only makes sense if Satan himself causes that evil (e.g., whispering). If Allah were the sole creator of that evil, Satan would have no part in it, and seeking refuge from him would be pointless.

5. The term 'shaytan' is derived from roots implying distance or deviation (or burning), and in either case it is a term of blame. Blame is only appropriate for a being with genuine power to act. If Satan has no power, blaming him would be unjust.

6. Similarly, 'al-rajim' (the accursed/stoned) indicates punishment, which is only appropriate for a being capable of action. If Satan is passive, such a description would be meaningless.

After these six proofs, the author anticipates an objection from the Jabriyya who might claim they do not hold pure Jabr but rather the theory of kasb (acquisition). The author dismisses this as empty: either the servant truly has the ability to originate his actions (in which case it is the Ash'ari position?) or he does not (in which case the same criticisms apply). There is no middle ground, and invoking 'kasb' without genuine power is mere rhetoric.

The passage concludes with a statement from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, though the text cuts off mid-sentence. The overall argument is that the very phrase used by Muslims in daily worship presupposes human agency, thereby refuting extreme determinism.

Key Terms
شيطان
شطن
شاط
ذم
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'shaytan' is a proper name with no inherent meaning about agency.
Correction: The argument shows that 'shaytan' is derived from roots that imply blame, and blame requires agency, so Satan must have power.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the fifth argument's claim that Satan must have power?
A) The Qur'an says so explicitly.
B) The word 'shaytan' is a noun of blame, and blame requires capability.
C) Satan is a powerful being by nature.
D) The Prophet said so.

Key Takeaway
The very word 'shaytan' is a term of blame that logically implies Satan has power over his actions, refuting the Jabri denial of creature agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you blame yourself for a sin, do you truly believe you had the power to avoid it? Or do you secretly think you were compelled? This lesson argues that blame only makes sense if you had power. How does that change how you view your past failures? Are you willing to accept full responsibility for your choices, even the ones you regret?
?
The Qur'an blames shaytan and holds him accountable. Do you treat your own sins as choices you had power over? Or do you sometimes excuse yourself by feeling helpless? What would it mean to truly own your actions before Allah? Will you repent sincerely with the conviction that you could have done otherwise?
?
If blame presupposes power, then Allah's blame of you in the Qur'an is a testimony that you are capable of obeying. When you read verses of warning, do you feel empowered or threatened? How will you respond today to the call to change?
