Sixth Argument: The Term 'Rajim' Implies Punishment and Agency
Sixth argument from the phrase 'A'udhu' against Jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'anic description of Satan as 'al-rajim' (the accursed/stoned) indicates punishment, which is only meaningful if Satan is a capable agent.

Core Idea
Punishing someone for an act only makes sense if they had the power to avoid it.

The sixth and final argument from the phrase 'A'udhu' uses the word 'al-rajim' (the accursed or the one stoned). This term implies that Satan is punished, and punishment is only just if the punished being had the ability to choose otherwise. Thus, Satan must have genuine agency, refuting the Jabri position.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the term 'rajim' and its meaning.
2
Understand the implication
The term indicates punishment. Punishment is only appropriate for a being with power (qudra).
3
Apply the logic
Since Satan is punished, he must have agency, contradicting the idea that all acts are from Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من وجهه تمسكهم بالقرآن أن قالوا: القرآن من الباء من (بسم الله) إلى السين من قوله: «من الجنة والناس» ناطق بفساد مذهب الجبر. ونحن نشرح ذلك في سورة الفاتحة. ليقاس عليه الباقي. ورتبناه على فصول: الفصل الأول في المباحث المستنبطة من قولنا: (أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم). قالوا: هذا يدل على بطلان القول بالجبر من وجوه:
الأول: إن قولنا: (أعوذ) اعتراف يكون العبد فاعلاً. ولو كان فعل العبد مخلوقاً لله تعالى، لامتنع كونه فاعلاً للاستعاذة. لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، لأن الله تعالى إذا خلق ذلك الفعل، لم يقدر العبد على الامتناع منه، وإن لم يخلقه الله تعالى فيه، استحال منه الإقدام على تحصيله. فثبت: أن قوله: (أعوذ) اعتراف بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه.
الثاني: إن الاستعاذة بالله، إنما تعقل، لو لم يكن الله هو الفاعل للأمور التي منها يستعاذ. أما إذا كان الفاعل لها هو الله تعالى، امتنع أن يستعاذ به تعالى من تلك الأفعال. لأنه يصير كأنه استعاذ بالله، من الله، في الفعل الذي فعله الله تعالى.

?

?

?

Reply to the Kasb Objection: No Middle Ground between Agency and Compulsion
Reply to the kasb objection against the six arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Jabriyya's appeal to 'kasb' (acquisition) fails as a middle ground, because the servant either has the ability to originate acts or he does not.

Core Idea
Either you can originate acts or you cannot—'kasb' adds nothing.

After presenting six arguments, the author anticipates a common objection: the Jabriyya may say they do not believe in pure compulsion but in 'kasb' (acquisition), meaning the servant acquires the act while Allah creates it. The author dismisses this as mere wordplay, arguing that the servant either has the power to originate his act (which is the author's own position) or he does not (in which case all the previous criticisms apply). There is no genuine third option.

Guided Path
1
Read the reply
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the opponent's claim and the author's response.
2
Analyze the dichotomy
The author presents a clear either/or: either the servant can obtain and originate something, or he cannot. There is no intermediate.
3
Understand the dismissal
If the servant can originate, it is the author's doctrine (i.e., the Mu'tazili view that humans have power). If not, the same arguments against Jabr apply. The term 'kasb' is dismissed as 'pure forgery'.

Source Text

The second type of their adherence to the Qur'an: they said: The Qur'an from the ba' of (Bismillah) to the sin of His saying: 'min al-jinnati wa al-nas' speaks of the corruption of the doctrine of Jabr. And we explain that in Surat al-Fatihah, for the rest to be measured by it. And we arranged it into chapters: The first chapter on the investigations derived from our saying: (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). They said: This indicates the falsity of the statement of Jabr from aspects:
The first: That our saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that the servant is an agent. And if the act of the servant were created by Allah Almighty, it would be impossible for him to be an agent of seeking refuge. Because obtaining what has already been obtained is impossible, for if Allah Almighty created that act, the servant would not be able to refrain from it, and if Allah did not create it in him, it would be impossible for him to proceed to obtain it. So it is established: that his saying: (I seek refuge) is an acknowledgment that he is the originator of his own acts.
The second: That seeking refuge in Allah is only conceivable if Allah is not the doer of the things from which refuge is sought. But if the doer of them is Allah Almighty, it is impossible to seek refuge in Him from those acts. Because it becomes as if he sought refuge in Allah from Allah, in the act that Allah Almighty did.
The third: That the servant's proceeding to seek refuge from sins indicates that the servant is not pleased with them. And if sins were created by Allah Almighty, it would necessarily follow that the servant is not pleased with Allah's decree. And that is disbelief.
The fourth: That seeking refuge in Allah from Satan is only conceivable if whispering is an act of Satan. But if it is an act of Allah Almighty, and Satan has no effect in it, how can it be conceived to seek refuge from the evil of Satan?
The fifth: That the word 'Satan' is derived. Either from 'shatana' which means distance, or from 'shata' which means 'al-ihtiraf' (deviation/burning). In both cases it is a noun of blame. And blame is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act. So if Satan has no power over the act, his blame is not appropriate.
The sixth: That 'al-rajim' means the one who is stoned. And it is an indication of his being punished. And that is only appropriate regarding one who is capable of the act, not regarding one who is incapable of it.
So it is established by what we have mentioned: that his saying (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan) invalidates the statement of Jabr. And let it not be said: We do not say Jabr, rather we say the servant is an acquirer (muktasib). For we say: This is nothing. For the servant either can obtain and originate something, or he cannot. If he can, then it is our doctrine. And if he cannot, then the aforementioned questions are directed. And there is no intermediate between negation and affirmation. So it is established: that canceling the word 'kasb' in the matter is pure forgery.
Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: If the aspects that they derived from our saying:
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument refuting the doctrine of Jabr (absolute determinism) by drawing six rational and linguistic proofs from the Qur'anic phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytani al-rajim' (I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan). The author first explains that the Qur'an from its very beginning refutes Jabr, and he focuses on this specific phrase, which will serve as a paradigm for other passages. The six arguments proceed as follows:

1. The act of saying 'I seek refuge' is an acknowledgment that the servant is the doer of his own action. If Allah had created that act, the servant could not be its agent, because either Allah creates it and the servant cannot avoid it (so he has no choice), or Allah does not create it and the servant cannot originate it (so he cannot act). Thus, the phrase proves human agency.

2. Seeking refuge in Allah from something only makes sense if Allah is not the doer of that thing. If Allah were the doer of the evil from which refuge is sought, the act of seeking refuge would become a request for protection from Allah's own act—an absurdity.

3. The servant's seeking refuge from sins shows he is displeased with them. If sins were created by Allah, then being displeased with them would imply being displeased with Allah's decree, which is tantamount to disbelief (kufr).

4. Seeking refuge from Satan's evil only makes sense if Satan himself causes that evil (e.g., whispering). If Allah were the sole creator of that evil, Satan would have no part in it, and seeking refuge from him would be pointless.

5. The term 'shaytan' is derived from roots implying distance or deviation (or burning), and in either case it is a term of blame. Blame is only appropriate for a being with genuine power to act. If Satan has no power, blaming him would be unjust.

6. Similarly, 'al-rajim' (the accursed/stoned) indicates punishment, which is only appropriate for a being capable of action. If Satan is passive, such a description would be meaningless.

After these six proofs, the author anticipates an objection from the Jabriyya who might claim they do not hold pure Jabr but rather the theory of kasb (acquisition). The author dismisses this as empty: either the servant truly has the ability to originate his actions (in which case it is the Ash'ari position?) or he does not (in which case the same criticisms apply). There is no middle ground, and invoking 'kasb' without genuine power is mere rhetoric.

The passage concludes with a statement from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, though the text cuts off mid-sentence. The overall argument is that the very phrase used by Muslims in daily worship presupposes human agency, thereby refuting extreme determinism.

Key Terms
كسب
جبر
تحصيل
تكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concept of 'kasb' provides a valid middle path between Jabr and free will, where Allah creates the act and the servant acquires it.
Correction: The author argues that 'kasb' without genuine power to originate is meaningless. Either the servant has the power (the author's view) or he does not (Jabr), and there is no viable third option.

Checkpoint
How does the author dismiss the opponent's appeal to 'kasb'?
A) By proving that 'kasb' is a Qur'anic term.
B) By arguing that 'kasb' is just a word that does not change the reality of the servant's inability.
C) By accepting 'kasb' as a middle ground.
D) By redefining 'kasb' as creation.

Key Takeaway
The claim of 'kasb' is a verbal evasion that does not escape the logical dichotomy: either humans truly originate acts or they are compelled, and the six arguments show compulsion is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says that claiming 'kasb' without real power is 'pure forgery'. Are there areas in your life where you use religious language or vague beliefs to avoid taking real responsibility for your choices? When have you made excuses instead of admitting you had the power to act differently?
?
The dichotomy is clear: either you can originate an act or you cannot. Do you truly believe that you have real agency in your moral decisions, or do you sometimes fall back on a sense that 'Allah made me do it' without question? What would change if you fully embraced the responsibility of your choices?
?
The author calls the middle ground 'nothing'. Is there a comfortable middle ground in your own thinking—a belief or habit that lets you avoid a hard truth? What would it cost you to face that truth directly?

The Absurdity of Seeking Refuge from Allah
Two proofs from isti'adhah refuting jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why seeking refuge from sins would be absurd if Allah were the sole creator of actions, and how this refutes determinism.

Core Idea
Seeking refuge from Allah's own acts is impossible — this disproves determinism.

The Qur'anic phrase 'A'udhu billahi min ash-shaytani r-rajim' is not just a ritual; it contains profound theological implications. The author presents a series of arguments showing that this formula cannot be reconciled with the doctrine of determinism (jabr). In this lesson, we examine the second and third arguments, which focus on the logical contradiction of seeking refuge from something that Allah Himself has created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a sense of the two arguments.
Focus on the logic: if Allah creates all acts, seeking refuge from evil acts becomes seeking refuge from Allah's own doing.
2
Identify the two arguments
Notice how the second argument deals with the intelligibility of seeking refuge, and the third with the slave's displeasure with divine decree.
Each argument is introduced by a number (second, third).
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why the third argument leads to disbelief (kufr) if sins are created by Allah.
The argument shows that one cannot be pleased with Allah's decree while simultaneously seeking refuge from what He decreed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الاستعاذة بالله ، إنما تعقل ، لو لم يكن الله هو الفاعل للأمور التي منها يستعاذ . أما إذا كان الفاعل لها هو الله تعالى ، امتنع أن يستعاذ به تعالى من تلك الأفعال. لأنه يصير كأنه استعاذ بالله ، من الله ، في الفعل الذي فعله الله تعالى . الثالث : إن إقدام العبد على الاستعاذة من المعاصي ، يدل على أن العبد غير راضي بها . ولو كانت المعاصي مخلوقة الله تعالى ، لزم أن لا يكون العبد راضيا بقضاء الله . وذلك كفر . الرابع : إن الاستعاذة بالله من الشيطان ، إنما تعقل لو كانت الوسوسة فعلاً للشيطان. أما إذا كانت فعلا الله تعالى ، ولم يكن للشيطان فيها أثر . فكيف يعقل أن يستعاذ من شر الشيطان ؟ الخامس : إن لفظ الشيطان ، مشتق . إما من شطن ، وهو البعد . أو من شاط ، وهو الاحتراف . وعلى التقديرين فهو اسم ذم . والذم إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل . فإذا كان الشيطان لا قدرة له على الفعل لم يحسن ذمه . السادس : إن ( الرجيم ) معناه المرجوم. وهو إشارة إلى كونه معذباً . وذلك إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل . لا في حق العاجز عنه . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قوله ( أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم ) : يبطل القول بالجبر . ولا يقال : إنا لا نقول بالجبر ، بل نقول : العبد مكتسب . لأنا نقول : هذا ليس بشيء . فإن العبد إما أن يمكنه تحصيل شيء وتكوينه ، أو لا يمكنه ذلك . فإن أمكنه فهو مذهبنا . وإن كان لا يمكنه فقد توجهت السؤالات المذكورة . وليس بين النفي والإثبات واسطة . فثبت : أن إلغاء لفظ الكسب في الشيء : محض التزوير
The second: Seeking refuge with Allah is only intelligible if Allah is not the doer of the things from which refuge is sought. But if the doer of them is Allah Almighty, it is impossible to seek refuge with Him from those acts, because it would be as if one is seeking refuge with Allah from Allah in the act that Allah has done. The third: The slave's undertaking to seek refuge from sins indicates that the slave is not pleased with them. And if sins were created by Allah Almighty, it would necessarily follow that the slave is not pleased with the decree of Allah, and that is disbelief. The fourth: Seeking refuge with Allah from the satan is only intelligible if the whispering were an act of the satan. But if it is an act of Allah Almighty and the satan has no effect in it, how can it be intelligible to seek refuge from the evil of the satan? The fifth: The word 'satan' is derived either from 'shatuna' (to be distant) or from 'shatā' (to burn), and in both meanings it is a term of blame. Blame is only appropriate in the case of one who is capable of action. So if the satan has no power to act, blaming him is not appropriate. The sixth: 'Rajim' means 'stoned', and it indicates that he is tormented. That is only appropriate in the case of one who is capable of action, not in the case of one who is incapable. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the saying 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan' invalidates the doctrine of determinism (jabr). And let it not be said: 'We do not affirm determinism, rather we say the slave is an acquirer (muktasib).' For we say: This is nothing. Because the slave either can acquire something and bring it into existence, or he cannot. If he can, that is our doctrine. If he cannot, then the aforementioned questions apply. There is no intermediate between negation and affirmation. So it is established that the employment of the term 'acquisition' (kash) in this matter is pure fabrication.

Commentary
This passage presents six numbered arguments (the second through sixth; the first is presumably on the previous page) to demonstrate that the Qur'anic formula 'A'udhu billahi min ash-shaytani r-rajim' refutes the doctrine of determinism (jabr). The author proceeds argument by argument. The second argument: If Allah were the sole doer of evil acts, seeking refuge from them would mean seeking refuge from Allah's own actions, which is absurd. The third: If sins were created by Allah, the person seeking refuge would be displeased with Allah's decree, which implies disbelief. The fourth: If Satan's whispers are actually created by Allah, seeking refuge from Satan makes no sense because Satan has no role. The fifth: The word 'satan' implies blameworthiness, which only applies to a voluntary agent; a non-voluntary agent cannot be blamed. The sixth: The word 'rajim' indicates punishment, which again presupposes capacity. After these arguments, the author concludes that the formula invalidates determinism. He then anticipates and refutes the objection that the acquisition (kash) theory saves the determinist position. He argues that either the slave can 'acquire' an act (make it his own) or not; if yes, that is the Ash'ari position (the author's own), if not, the same objections apply. There is no middle ground, so 'acquisition' is mere fabrication.

الاستعاذة
الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think isti'adhah is merely a formula with no theological content.
Correction: The author demonstrates that it contains a rational argument against determinism; if one uses it, they implicitly reject jabr.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why is seeking refuge from evil acts impossible if Allah is the doer of all acts?
A) Because it would mean seeking refuge from Allah's mercy
B) Because it would mean seeking refuge from Allah's own action
C) Because it would mean denying Allah's power
D) Because it would imply dualism

Key Takeaway
The isti'adhah formula refutes determinism because seeking refuge from sin would be either absurd (if Allah creates sins) or imply disbelief in divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'a'udhu billahi min ash-shaytan ir-rajim', do you truly feel that Allah is your refuge from evil, or do you sometimes think that He is the One who decreed that evil? How does this lesson force you to reconcile your belief in divine decree with your instinct to seek protection?
?
Is there any area of your life where you are secretly displeased with Allah's decree—perhaps a trial, a loss, or a delay? The third argument equates displeasure with sins (if from Allah) to disbelief. How does this challenge you to examine your heart's contentment with qada?
?
The second argument shows that seeking refuge from Allah's own act is absurd. Are there moments when your du'a or actions imply that you are 'running from' something that Allah has written? How can you transform your isti'adhah into a deeper trust in Allah's wisdom and justice?

The Role of Satan and the Necessity of Moral Agency
Three arguments from isti'adhah regarding Satan's agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the isti'adhah formula requires Satan to be a genuine agent, and how the derived terms 'shaytan' and 'rajim' imply blame and punishability.

Core Idea
Blame and punishment only make sense for a voluntary agent — Satan must act.

The isti'adhah formula also mentions Satan. The author uses linguistic and theological analysis to show that the words 'shaytan' and 'rajim' presuppose that Satan is capable of action. If determinism were true, Satan would be powerless, and blaming him would be meaningless. This lesson explores the fourth, fifth, and sixth arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the three arguments about Satan and blame.
Notice how each argument focuses on a different aspect: the act of whispering, the etymology of 'shaytan', and the term 'rajim'.
2
Analyze each argument
For each argument, identify the key premise and conclusion. The fourth links seeking refuge to Satan's agency, the fifth links blame to capacity, the sixth links punishment to capacity.
All three converge on the idea that moral language requires free will.
3
Connect to the overall refutation
Think about how these arguments collectively show that the isti'adhah formula is incompatible with determinism.
If everything is from Allah, Satan is not a real agent and blame/punishment are unjust.

Source Text

The Final Verdict: Isti'adhah Invalidates Determinism and the Kasb Theory
Conclusion and refutation of the kasb theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author concludes his argument and refutes the acquisition theory (kasb) as an attempt to reconcile determinism with human responsibility.

Core Idea
The acquisition theory fails: either the slave can create acts or not — no middle ground.

After presenting six arguments, the author asserts that the isti'adhah formula decisively invalidates determinism (jabr). He then anticipates the objection that the acquisition (kasb) theory offers an escape. This lesson examines his powerful refutation, which exposes the logical flaw in the kasb position: it either collapses into free will or remains determinism by another name.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding statement
Read the first part: 'فثبت بما ذكرنا ... يبطل القول بالجبر' — the author declares that the six arguments have established that isti'adhah invalidates jabr.
Note that this is the summary of the previous arguments.
2
Analyze the objection and refutation
Read the objection: 'ولا يقال ... مكتسب' — some might say they deny jabr but affirm acquisition (kasb). Then read the refutation: the two possibilities (can or cannot bring about existence).
Identify the logical disjunction: either the slave can effect something or not.
3
Understand the 'no intermediate' principle
Reflect on the statement 'وليس بين النفي والإثبات واسطة' — there is no middle ground between affirming the slave's power and denying it.
This is a crucial logical principle: the term 'kasb' tries to create a third option but fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الاستعاذة بالله ، إنما تعقل ، لو لم يكن الله هو الفاعل للأمور التي منها يستعاذ . أما إذا كان الفاعل لها هو الله تعالى ، امتنع أن يستعاذ به تعالى من تلك الأفعال. لأنه يصير كأنه استعاذ بالله ، من الله ، في الفعل الذي فعله الله تعالى . الثالث : إن إقدام العبد على الاستعاذة من المعاصي ، يدل على أن العبد غير راضي بها . ولو كانت المعاصي مخلوقة الله تعالى ، لزم أن لا يكون العبد راضيا بقضاء الله . وذلك كفر . الرابع : إن الاستعاذة بالله من الشيطان ، إنما تعقل لو كانت الوسوسة فعلاً للشيطان. أما إذا كانت فعلا الله تعالى ، ولم يكن للشيطان فيها أثر . فكيف يعقل أن يستعاذ من شر الشيطان ؟ الخامس : إن لفظ الشيطان ، مشتق . إما من شطن ، وهو البعد . أو من شاط ، وهو الاحتراف . وعلى التقديرين فهو اسم ذم . والذم إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل . فإذا كان الشيطان لا قدرة له على الفعل لم يحسن ذمه . السادس : إن ( الرجيم ) معناه المرجوم. وهو إشارة إلى كونه معذباً . وذلك إنما يحسن في حق القادر على الفعل . لا في حق العاجز عنه . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قوله ( أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم ) : يبطل القول بالجبر . ولا يقال : إنا لا نقول بالجبر ، بل نقول : العبد مكتسب . لأنا نقول : هذا ليس بشيء . فإن العبد إما أن يمكنه تحصيل شيء وتكوينه ، أو لا يمكنه ذلك . فإن أمكنه فهو مذهبنا . وإن كان لا يمكنه فقد توجهت السؤالات المذكورة . وليس بين النفي والإثبات واسطة . فثبت : أن إلغاء لفظ الكسب في الشيء : محض التزوير
The second: Seeking refuge with Allah is only intelligible if Allah is not the doer of the things from which refuge is sought. But if the doer of them is Allah Almighty, it is impossible to seek refuge with Him from those acts, because it would be as if one is seeking refuge with Allah from Allah in the act that Allah has done. The third: The slave's undertaking to seek refuge from sins indicates that the slave is not pleased with them. And if sins were created by Allah Almighty, it would necessarily follow that the slave is not pleased with the decree of Allah, and that is disbelief. The fourth: Seeking refuge with Allah from the satan is only intelligible if the whispering were an act of the satan. But if it is an act of Allah Almighty and the satan has no effect in it, how can it be intelligible to seek refuge from the evil of the satan? The fifth: The word 'satan' is derived either from 'shatuna' (to be distant) or from 'shatā' (to burn), and in both meanings it is a term of blame. Blame is only appropriate in the case of one who is capable of action. So if the satan has no power to act, blaming him is not appropriate. The sixth: 'Rajim' means 'stoned', and it indicates that he is tormented. That is only appropriate in the case of one who is capable of action, not in the case of one who is incapable. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the saying 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan' invalidates the doctrine of determinism (jabr). And let it not be said: 'We do not affirm determinism, rather we say the slave is an acquirer (muktasib).' For we say: This is nothing. Because the slave either can acquire something and bring it into existence, or he cannot. If he can, that is our doctrine. If he cannot, then the aforementioned questions apply. There is no intermediate between negation and affirmation. So it is established that the employment of the term 'acquisition' (kash) in this matter is pure fabrication.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents six numbered arguments (the second through sixth; the first is presumably on the previous page) to demonstrate that the Qur'anic formula 'A'udhu billahi min ash-shaytani r-rajim' refutes the doctrine of determinism (jabr). The author proceeds argument by argument. The second argument: If Allah were the sole doer of evil acts, seeking refuge from them would mean seeking refuge from Allah's own actions, which is absurd. The third: If sins were created by Allah, the person seeking refuge would be displeased with Allah's decree, which implies disbelief. The fourth: If Satan's whispers are actually created by Allah, seeking refuge from Satan makes no sense because Satan has no role. The fifth: The word 'satan' implies blameworthiness, which only applies to a voluntary agent; a non-voluntary agent cannot be blamed. The sixth: The word 'rajim' indicates punishment, which again presupposes capacity. After these arguments, the author concludes that the formula invalidates determinism. He then anticipates and refutes the objection that the acquisition (kash) theory saves the determinist position. He argues that either the slave can 'acquire' an act (make it his own) or not; if yes, that is the Ash'ari position (the author's own), if not, the same objections apply. There is no middle ground, so 'acquisition' is mere fabrication.

Key Terms
الجبر
الكسب
المكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the acquisition theory is a coherent third way between free will and determinism.
Correction: The author shows it is logically either one or the other; 'kasb' is a meaningless term.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what does the author consider as the only two possibilities for the slave's action?
A) The slave either creates his own acts or Allah creates all
B) The slave either acquires the act or does nothing
C) The slave either can bring about something or cannot
D) The slave either sins or obeys

Key Takeaway
The kasb theory is exposed as a verbal trick that fails to resolve the logical dilemma; the isti'adhah formula necessitates real human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan, do you genuinely believe that you have the power to turn to Allah by your own will? If your refuge-seeking itself is created by Allah, then how is it any different from being compelled?
?
Think of a sin you committed recently. Did you truly have the ability to avoid it? If not, then why do you feel remorse? The kasb theory would say you 'acquired' the sin—but if you could not have done otherwise, is your guilt real?
?
The author says there is no middle ground: either you affirm real human agency or you don't. In your heart, which do you truly believe? And does your belief match how you live—do you blame qadr for your failures or take ownership?

Introduction: Isti'adhah Refutes Both Jabr and I'tizal
Ahl as-Sunnah introduces four arguments from isti'adhah against Mu'tazilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ahl as-Sunnah uses the same isti'adhah formula to refute both determinism (jabr) and Mu'tazilism (i'tizal), establishing their balanced position.

Core Idea
The same phrase can refute two opposite extremes.

In this lesson, we begin a series of arguments extracted from the simple phrase 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan.' The Ahl as-Sunnah notes that if this phrase undermines determinism, it also undermines the Mu'tazilite view of free will. The introduction sets the stage for four logical arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory statement
Read the Arabic and translation of the opening sentence. Notice how the author frames the discussion as a parallel refutation.
This is a classic kalam tactic: using the opponent's own evidence against them.
2
Identify the two schools mentioned
The passage mentions 'jabr' (determinism) and 'i'tizal' (Mu'tazilism). Understand that these are two opposing views on divine will and human agency.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why the same phrase can refute both extremes. This shows that the Ahl as-Sunnah's position is a middle path.
This will be developed in the following lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : إن كانت الوجوه التي استنبطوها من قولنا : ( أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم ) توجب فساد الجبر ، فههنا وجوه أخرى مستنبطة منها توجب فساد القول بالاعتزال . فالأول : إن شر الشياطين والأبالسة إذا لم يكن الله تعالى فيه أثر البتة ، لم تجز الاستعاذة بالله . لأن على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون المطلوب من الله في هذه الاستعاذة : أن يمنع الشيطان بالنبي والزجر والتهديد ، أو بأن يمنعه منه بالقهر والإلجاء . أما الأول فقد فعله الله تعالى ، فكان طلبه لتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو عبث . وإن كان الثاني لم يجز من الله تعالى أن يفعله ، لأن الإلجاء ينافي كون الشيطان مكلفاً . الثاني : إن الله تعالى إما أن يكون مريداً لصلاح حال العبد ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . فإن كان الأول فالشيطان إما أن يتوقع منه إفساد العبد ، أو لا يتوقع منه ذلك . فإن كان المتوقع من الشيطان إفساد العبد ، أن الله تعالى يريد إصلاحه ، فلم خلقه ؟ ولم يسلطه على العبد ؟ وإن كان لا يتوقع منه إفساد العبد . فأي فائدة في الاستعاذة بالله منه ؟ وأما الثاني : فهو أن الله قد لا يريد إصلاح العبد ، وعلى هذا التقدير : الاستعاذة بالله لا تفيد الاعتصام من آفات الدين والدنيا . الثالث : إن الشيطان إما أن يكون مجبورا على فعل الشر ، أو لا يكون قادراً على الشر والخير. فإن كان الأول فقد أجبره الله تعالى على الشر . وذلك يناقض الاعتزال. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لا تترجح فاعلية الشر على فاعلية الخير، إلا لمرجح من قبل الله تعالى - على ما قررناه مراراً وأطواراً - فحينئذ يلزم الجبر . فكيف يستعاذ من الشيطان ؟ الرابع : إن كان الفعل المستعاذ منه : معلوم الوقوع . فهو واجب
The Ahl as-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah say: If the arguments that they derived from our saying 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan' necessitate the corruption of determinism (jabr), then here are other arguments derived from it that necessitate the corruption of the doctrine of Mu'tazilism (i'tizal). The first: If the evil of the devils and satans has absolutely no effect from Allah, then seeking refuge with Allah is not permissible. Because on this estimation, either what is sought from Allah in this seeking refuge is that He prevent the satan by admonition, reprimand, and threat, or that He prevent him by compulsion and coercion. As for the first, Allah has already done it, so requesting it would be requesting what has already been obtained, which is futile. And if the second, it is not permissible for Allah to do it, because compulsion contradicts the satan being obligated (mukallaf). The second: Allah Almighty either wills the good state of the slave, or He does not. If the first, then the satan is either expected to corrupt the slave or not. If the satan is expected to corrupt the slave while Allah wills his reformation, why did He create him? And why did He give him power over the slave? And if the satan is not expected to corrupt the slave, what benefit is there in seeking refuge with Allah from him? As for the second, that Allah may not will the reformation of the slave, and on this estimation, seeking refuge with Allah does not provide protection from the afflictions of religion and world. The third: The satan is either compelled (majbur) to do evil or he is capable of both evil and good. If the first, then Allah has compelled him to evil, and that contradicts Mu'tazilism. If the second, then at that time the performance of evil is not more preponderant over the performance of good except by a preponderator (murajjih) from Allah—as we have established repeatedly and in many ways—then determinism (jabr) becomes necessary. So how can one seek refuge from the satan? The fourth: If the act from which refuge is sought is known to occur, then it is necessary (wajib).
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments showing that the same isti'adhah formula that refutes determinism also refutes Mu'tazilism (i'tizal). The Ahl as-Sunnah states that if the previous arguments refuted jabr, here are parallel arguments against the Mu'tazilites. The first argument: If Satan's evil has no part in Allah's action, then seeking refuge from Satan is either asking Allah to reprimand him (already done, so futile) or to compel him (impossible because compulsion negates moral responsibility). The second: If Allah wills the slave's good but Satan can corrupt him, why did Allah create Satan? If Allah does not will the slave's good, seeking refuge is useless. The third: Satan is either compelled to evil (contradicting Mu'tazilite belief in free will) or has free will—but then a preponderator from Allah is needed to tip the scale toward evil, leading to determinism. Either way, Mu'tazilism fails. The fourth: If the act is foreknown to occur, it becomes necessary (wajib), implying determinism. Thus the isti'adhah undermines the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
الاعتزال
الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the isti'adhah phrase only supports one side of the qadar debate. Actually, the author shows it can refute both extremes.
Correction: The phrase is used as a neutral tool to expose inconsistencies in both determinist and free-will positions.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the isti'adhah phrase refute?
A) Only determinism (jabr)
B) Only Mu'tazilism (i'tizal)
C) Both determinism and Mu'tazilism
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The isti'adhah formula serves as a common ground that can refute both jabr and i'tizal, revealing the middle path of Ahl as-Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I seek refuge with Allah,' do you feel it as a confession of your own weakness and need, or as a mechanical habit? What would change if you truly felt that this phrase holds the power to correct both your pride and your despair?
?
Think of a time when you blamed Allah for a hardship you faced (a jabr-like attitude) or a time when you felt your success was purely your own doing (an i'tizal-like attitude). Which extreme inclines your heart more often? Right now, commit to reciting 'A'udhu billahi min ash-shaytan ir-rajim' as a reminder that you are neither forced nor self-sufficient, but utterly reliant on Him.

First Argument: Seeking Refuge from Satan's Evil Is Futile If Allah Has No Role
First argument: The dilemma of seeking refuge from Satan if Allah is uninvolved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first logical argument that shows how seeking refuge from Satan becomes either unnecessary or impossible if Allah has no part in preventing his evil.

Core Idea
If Allah can't affect Satan, then refuge-seeking is either futile or impossible.

The first argument targets the Mu'tazilite assumption that Satan's evil occurs without any act from Allah. The author breaks down the two possible ways Allah could prevent Satan: through reprimand (already done) or through compulsion (negates moral responsibility). Either way, seeking refuge is illogical.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the first argument. Notice the two alternatives presented: either Allah prevents by reprimand or by compulsion.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
2
Analyze the first option (reprimand)
Understand why asking for reprimand is futile: Allah has already done it. The argument says 'talab li tahsil al-hasil' - requesting what already exists.
3
Analyze the second option (compulsion)
Understand why compulsion is impossible: it contradicts Satan being mukallaf (morally responsible). This refutes Mu'tazilism because they believe in human free will.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Conclude that the Mu'tazilite position leads to a contradiction: one cannot consistently seek refuge if Allah has no role in preventing evil.
This argument is parallel to the one against jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : إن كانت الوجوه التي استنبطوها من قولنا : ( أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم ) توجب فساد الجبر ، فههنا وجوه أخرى مستنبطة منها توجب فساد القول بالاعتزال . فالأول : إن شر الشياطين والأبالسة إذا لم يكن الله تعالى فيه أثر البتة ، لم تجز الاستعاذة بالله . لأن على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون المطلوب من الله في هذه الاستعاذة : أن يمنع الشيطان بالنبي والزجر والتهديد ، أو بأن يمنعه منه بالقهر والإلجاء . أما الأول فقد فعله الله تعالى ، فكان طلبه لتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو عبث . وإن كان الثاني لم يجز من الله تعالى أن يفعله ، لأن الإلجاء ينافي كون الشيطان مكلفاً . الثاني : إن الله تعالى إما أن يكون مريداً لصلاح حال العبد ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . فإن كان الأول فالشيطان إما أن يتوقع منه إفساد العبد ، أو لا يتوقع منه ذلك . فإن كان المتوقع من الشيطان إفساد العبد ، أن الله تعالى يريد إصلاحه ، فلم خلقه ؟ ولم يسلطه على العبد ؟ وإن كان لا يتوقع منه إفساد العبد . فأي فائدة في الاستعاذة بالله منه ؟ وأما الثاني : فهو أن الله قد لا يريد إصلاح العبد ، وعلى هذا التقدير : الاستعاذة بالله لا تفيد الاعتصام من آفات الدين والدنيا . الثالث : إن الشيطان إما أن يكون مجبورا على فعل الشر ، أو لا يكون قادراً على الشر والخير. فإن كان الأول فقد أجبره الله تعالى على الشر . وذلك يناقض الاعتزال. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لا تترجح فاعلية الشر على فاعلية الخير، إلا لمرجح من قبل الله تعالى - على ما قررناه مراراً وأطواراً - فحينئذ يلزم الجبر . فكيف يستعاذ من الشيطان ؟ الرابع : إن كان الفعل المستعاذ منه : معلوم الوقوع . فهو واجب
The Ahl as-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah say: If the arguments that they derived from our saying 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan' necessitate the corruption of determinism (jabr), then here are other arguments derived from it that necessitate the corruption of the doctrine of Mu'tazilism (i'tizal). The first: If the evil of the devils and satans has absolutely no effect from Allah, then seeking refuge with Allah is not permissible. Because on this estimation, either what is sought from Allah in this seeking refuge is that He prevent the satan by admonition, reprimand, and threat, or that He prevent him by compulsion and coercion. As for the first, Allah has already done it, so requesting it would be requesting what has already been obtained, which is futile. And if the second, it is not permissible for Allah to do it, because compulsion contradicts the satan being obligated (mukallaf). The second: Allah Almighty either wills the good state of the slave, or He does not. If the first, then the satan is either expected to corrupt the slave or not. If the satan is expected to corrupt the slave while Allah wills his reformation, why did He create him? And why did He give him power over the slave? And if the satan is not expected to corrupt the slave, what benefit is there in seeking refuge with Allah from him? As for the second, that Allah may not will the reformation of the slave, and on this estimation, seeking refuge with Allah does not provide protection from the afflictions of religion and world. The third: The satan is either compelled (majbur) to do evil or he is capable of both evil and good. If the first, then Allah has compelled him to evil, and that contradicts Mu'tazilism. If the second, then at that time the performance of evil is not more preponderant over the performance of good except by a preponderator (murajjih) from Allah—as we have established repeatedly and in many ways—then determinism (jabr) becomes necessary. So how can one seek refuge from the satan? The fourth: If the act from which refuge is sought is known to occur, then it is necessary (wajib).
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments showing that the same isti'adhah formula that refutes determinism also refutes Mu'tazilism (i'tizal). The Ahl as-Sunnah states that if the previous arguments refuted jabr, here are parallel arguments against the Mu'tazilites. The first argument: If Satan's evil has no part in Allah's action, then seeking refuge from Satan is either asking Allah to reprimand him (already done, so futile) or to compel him (impossible because compulsion negates moral responsibility). The second: If Allah wills the slave's good but Satan can corrupt him, why did Allah create Satan? If Allah does not will the slave's good, seeking refuge is useless. The third: Satan is either compelled to evil (contradicting Mu'tazilite belief in free will) or has free will—but then a preponderator from Allah is needed to tip the scale toward evil, leading to determinism. Either way, Mu'tazilism fails. The fourth: If the act is foreknown to occur, it becomes necessary (wajib), implying determinism. Thus the isti'adhah undermines the Mu'tazilite position.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that seeking refuge from Satan is always valid regardless of theological framework. The argument shows it depends on divine action.
Correction: If Allah has no role, the act of seeking refuge becomes inherently contradictory.

Checkpoint
What is the problem with the second option (prevention by compulsion)?
A) It is impossible for Allah to do
B) It contradicts Satan being mukallaf (morally responsible)
C) Allah has already done it
D) It is not effective

Key Takeaway
The first argument uses a dilemma to show that if Allah has no effect on Satan's evil, seeking refuge is either redundant or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'A'udhu billahi min ash-shaytan ir-rajim', do you truly believe that Allah is the One who protects you from Satan, or do you say it out of habit? What would change in your heart if you genuinely internalized that without Allah's active role, your refuge is futile?
?
The Mu'tazilites denied Allah's effect on evil to protect free will, but this argument shows the consequence. Are there areas in your life where you resist acknowledging Allah's complete control over all things, including evil, because you want to preserve a sense of autonomy? Can you consciously surrender that control to Him tonight?
?
The argument states that if Allah already admonished Satan, asking Him to do so again is futile. When you make du'a, do you ever ask for something that Allah has already granted in your deen, missing the deeper need for His direct intervention? How can you avoid 'requesting the already obtained' and instead ask for what only He can give?

Second Argument: The Problem of Allah's Will and Satan's Existence
Second argument: The problem of divine will in isti'adhah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second argument scrutinizes the Mu'tazilite view by analyzing whether Allah wills the slave's good and whether Satan can corrupt him.

Core Idea
If Allah wills good, why create a corrupting Satan? If He doesn't, refuge is useless.

The second argument examines the relationship between Allah's will and Satan's actions. It considers two scenarios: either Allah wills the slave's good or He does not. In each case, the existence of Satan and the act of seeking refuge become problematic. This argument exposes the incoherence of the Mu'tazilite denial of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the branching: two main possibilities regarding Allah's will, and sub-possibilities.
2
Trace the first branch: Allah wills good
If Allah wills good for the slave, then either Satan is expected to corrupt him or not. If expected, why create Satan? If not, why seek refuge?
This shows a contradiction.
3
Trace the second branch: Allah does not will good
If Allah does not will good, then seeking refuge is ineffective because it cannot protect from afflictions.
4
Formulate the contradiction
In both branches, the act of seeking refuge is problematic, showing the Mu'tazilite conception of divine will is flawed.

قال أهل السنة والجماعة : إن كانت الوجوه التي استنبطوها من قولنا : ( أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم ) توجب فساد الجبر ، فههنا وجوه أخرى مستنبطة منها توجب فساد القول بالاعتزال . فالأول : إن شر الشياطين والأبالسة إذا لم يكن الله تعالى فيه أثر البتة ، لم تجز الاستعاذة بالله . لأن على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون المطلوب من الله في هذه الاستعاذة : أن يمنع الشيطان بالنبي والزجر والتهديد ، أو بأن يمنعه منه بالقهر والإلجاء . أما الأول فقد فعله الله تعالى ، فكان طلبه لتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو عبث . وإن كان الثاني لم يجز من الله تعالى أن يفعله ، لأن الإلجاء ينافي كون الشيطان مكلفاً . الثاني : إن الله تعالى إما أن يكون مريداً لصلاح حال العبد ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . فإن كان الأول فالشيطان إما أن يتوقع منه إفساد العبد ، أو لا يتوقع منه ذلك . فإن كان المتوقع من الشيطان إفساد العبد ، أن الله تعالى يريد إصلاحه ، فلم خلقه ؟ ولم يسلطه على العبد ؟ وإن كان لا يتوقع منه إفساد العبد . فأي فائدة في الاستعاذة بالله منه ؟ وأما الثاني : فهو أن الله قد لا يريد إصلاح العبد ، وعلى هذا التقدير : الاستعاذة بالله لا تفيد الاعتصام من آفات الدين والدنيا . الثالث : إن الشيطان إما أن يكون مجبورا على فعل الشر ، أو لا يكون قادراً على الشر والخير. فإن كان الأول فقد أجبره الله تعالى على الشر . وذلك يناقض الاعتزال. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لا تترجح فاعلية الشر على فاعلية الخير، إلا لمرجح من قبل الله تعالى - على ما قررناه مراراً وأطواراً - فحينئذ يلزم الجبر . فكيف يستعاذ من الشيطان ؟ الرابع : إن كان الفعل المستعاذ منه : معلوم الوقوع . فهو واجب
The Ahl as-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah say: If the arguments that they derived from our saying 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan' necessitate the corruption of determinism (jabr), then here are other arguments derived from it that necessitate the corruption of the doctrine of Mu'tazilism (i'tizal). The first: If the evil of the devils and satans has absolutely no effect from Allah, then seeking refuge with Allah is not permissible. Because on this estimation, either what is sought from Allah in this seeking refuge is that He prevent the satan by admonition, reprimand, and threat, or that He prevent him by compulsion and coercion. As for the first, Allah has already done it, so requesting it would be requesting what has already been obtained, which is futile. And if the second, it is not permissible for Allah to do it, because compulsion contradicts the satan being obligated (mukallaf). The second: Allah Almighty either wills the good state of the slave, or He does not. If the first, then the satan is either expected to corrupt the slave or not. If the satan is expected to corrupt the slave while Allah wills his reformation, why did He create him? And why did He give him power over the slave? And if the satan is not expected to corrupt the slave, what benefit is there in seeking refuge with Allah from him? As for the second, that Allah may not will the reformation of the slave, and on this estimation, seeking refuge with Allah does not provide protection from the afflictions of religion and world. The third: The satan is either compelled (majbur) to do evil or he is capable of both evil and good. If the first, then Allah has compelled him to evil, and that contradicts Mu'tazilism. If the second, then at that time the performance of evil is not more preponderant over the performance of good except by a preponderator (murajjih) from Allah—as we have established repeatedly and in many ways—then determinism (jabr) becomes necessary. So how can one seek refuge from the satan? The fourth: If the act from which refuge is sought is known to occur, then it is necessary (wajib).
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments showing that the same isti'adhah formula that refutes determinism also refutes Mu'tazilism (i'tizal). The Ahl as-Sunnah states that if the previous arguments refuted jabr, here are parallel arguments against the Mu'tazilites. The first argument: If Satan's evil has no part in Allah's action, then seeking refuge from Satan is either asking Allah to reprimand him (already done, so futile) or to compel him (impossible because compulsion negates moral responsibility). The second: If Allah wills the slave's good but Satan can corrupt him, why did Allah create Satan? If Allah does not will the slave's good, seeking refuge is useless. The third: Satan is either compelled to evil (contradicting Mu'tazilite belief in free will) or has free will—but then a preponderator from Allah is needed to tip the scale toward evil, leading to determinism. Either way, Mu'tazilism fails. The fourth: If the act is foreknown to occur, it becomes necessary (wajib), implying determinism. Thus the isti'adhah undermines the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the problem if Allah wills good for the slave and Satan is expected to corrupt him?
A) Satan would be compelled
B) Why did Allah create Satan?
C) Refuge becomes mandatory
D) Allah would be unjust

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that the Mu'tazilite position fails to reconcile Allah's will with Satan's existence and the act of seeking refuge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek refuge from the accursed Satan, do you truly believe that Allah's will encompasses even the existence of this enemy? If He willed your reformation, why would He allow Satan to tempt you? How does this question challenge your reliance on Allah's wisdom? Will you today change your attitude towards adversity, accepting it as part of a divine plan you cannot fully comprehend?
?
If you were to face a situation where your faith is tested and you feel that Allah is not willing your good, what would that mean for your practice of seeking refuge? Would you stop? Or would you persist out of obedience, trusting that He is the only source of protection? Examine your heart: is your refuge conditional on feeling successful, or is it an act of submission regardless of outcome?
?
The argument shows that if Allah does not will reformation, then refuge is useless. Do you ever act as if your own efforts or luck protect you, rather than depending wholly on Allah? When you say 'A'udhu billah', do you feel the weight of that statement, acknowledging that only He can save you from both external and internal corruption? Commit to reciting it today with full presence of heart.

Third Argument: Satan's Capacity and the Need for a Preponderator
Third argument: Dilemma of Satan's capacity and determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that shows how the Mu'tazilite view leads to either compulsion or determinism when analyzing Satan's moral capacity.

Core Idea
If Satan has free will, a divine preponderator is needed—leading to jabr.

The third argument focuses on whether Satan is compelled in his evil actions or has free will. If compelled, then it contradicts Mu'tazilism. If he has free will, then a preponderator from Allah must tip the scale toward evil, which leads to determinism once again. Thus, the Mu'tazilite position is caught in a dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the two alternatives: Satan is compelled (majbur) or capable of both good and evil.
2
Examine the first alternative (compulsion)
If Satan is compelled, then Allah compels evil, which contradicts Mu'tazilite belief in free will.
3
Examine the second alternative (capable of both)
If Satan can do both, then for evil to preponderate there must be a preponderator (murajjih) from Allah, leading to determinism.
This is a standard kalam argument for determinism from the principle of sufficient reason.
4
Conclude the dilemma
Either way, the Mu'tazilite position is refuted, and the necessity of seeking refuge becomes problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : إن كانت الوجوه التي استنبطوها من قولنا : ( أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم ) توجب فساد الجبر ، فههنا وجوه أخرى مستنبطة منها توجب فساد القول بالاعتزال . فالأول : إن شر الشياطين والأبالسة إذا لم يكن الله تعالى فيه أثر البتة ، لم تجز الاستعاذة بالله . لأن على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون المطلوب من الله في هذه الاستعاذة : أن يمنع الشيطان بالنبي والزجر والتهديد ، أو بأن يمنعه منه بالقهر والإلجاء . أما الأول فقد فعله الله تعالى ، فكان طلبه لتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو عبث . وإن كان الثاني لم يجز من الله تعالى أن يفعله ، لأن الإلجاء ينافي كون الشيطان مكلفاً . الثاني : إن الله تعالى إما أن يكون مريداً لصلاح حال العبد ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . فإن كان الأول فالشيطان إما أن يتوقع منه إفساد العبد ، أو لا يتوقع منه ذلك . فإن كان المتوقع من الشيطان إفساد العبد ، أن الله تعالى يريد إصلاحه ، فلم خلقه ؟ ولم يسلطه على العبد ؟ وإن كان لا يتوقع منه إفساد العبد . فأي فائدة في الاستعاذة بالله منه ؟ وأما الثاني : فهو أن الله قد لا يريد إصلاح العبد ، وعلى هذا التقدير : الاستعاذة بالله لا تفيد الاعتصام من آفات الدين والدنيا . الثالث : إن الشيطان إما أن يكون مجبورا على فعل الشر ، أو لا يكون قادراً على الشر والخير. فإن كان الأول فقد أجبره الله تعالى على الشر . وذلك يناقض الاعتزال. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لا تترجح فاعلية الشر على فاعلية الخير، إلا لمرجح من قبل الله تعالى - على ما قررناه مراراً وأطواراً - فحينئذ يلزم الجبر . فكيف يستعاذ من الشيطان ؟ الرابع : إن كان الفعل المستعاذ منه : معلوم الوقوع . فهو واجب
The Ahl as-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah say: If the arguments that they derived from our saying 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed satan' necessitate the corruption of determinism (jabr), then here are other arguments derived from it that necessitate the corruption of the doctrine of Mu'tazilism (i'tizal). The first: If the evil of the devils and satans has absolutely no effect from Allah, then seeking refuge with Allah is not permissible. Because on this estimation, either what is sought from Allah in this seeking refuge is that He prevent the satan by admonition, reprimand, and threat, or that He prevent him by compulsion and coercion. As for the first, Allah has already done it, so requesting it would be requesting what has already been obtained, which is futile. And if the second, it is not permissible for Allah to do it, because compulsion contradicts the satan being obligated (mukallaf). The second: Allah Almighty either wills the good state of the slave, or He does not. If the first, then the satan is either expected to corrupt the slave or not. If the satan is expected to corrupt the slave while Allah wills his reformation, why did He create him? And why did He give him power over the slave? And if the satan is not expected to corrupt the slave, what benefit is there in seeking refuge with Allah from him? As for the second, that Allah may not will the reformation of the slave, and on this estimation, seeking refuge with Allah does not provide protection from the afflictions of religion and world. The third: The satan is either compelled (majbur) to do evil or he is capable of both evil and good. If the first, then Allah has compelled him to evil, and that contradicts Mu'tazilism. If the second, then at that time the performance of evil is not more preponderant over the performance of good except by a preponderator (murajjih) from Allah—as we have established repeatedly and in many ways—then determinism (jabr) becomes necessary. So how can one seek refuge from the satan? The fourth: If the act from which refuge is sought is known to occur, then it is necessary (wajib).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if Satan is capable of both, then he is free and Mu'tazilism is correct. But the argument shows that free choice alone does not explain why one action occurs; a preponderator is needed.
Correction: The principle of preponderance (tarjih) is a fundamental kalam concept: for any contingent choice to occur, there must be a determining factor.

Examine your heart: are there areas where you subtly assume you are the ultimate decider of your actions, ignoring the need for Allah's preponderator? What would it mean for you to truly submit to His absolute will?
?

Make a concrete commitment today to acknowledge His preponderance in every decision.

Fourth Argument: Foreknowledge and Necessity
Fourth argument: Foreknowledge entails necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth brief argument that uses the concept of foreknowledge to argue that acts from which refuge is sought are necessary, implying determinism.

Core Idea
If an act is foreknown, it becomes necessary.

The fourth argument is the shortest: it states that if the act from which refuge is sought is known to occur (by divine foreknowledge), then it is necessary (wajib). This implies that the act is determined, which undermines the Mu'tazilite commitment to contingency and free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument
Read the Arabic and translation. It's a single sentence: 'If the act from which refuge is sought is known to occur, then it is necessary.'
2
Understand 'known to occur'
This refers to divine foreknowledge: Allah knows everything that will happen. That knowledge is infallible.
3
Understand the implication of necessity
If something is foreknown, it cannot fail to occur; thus it becomes necessary (wajib). This is a standard theological determinism argument.
4
Connect to Mu'tazilism
Mu'tazilites believe in genuine contingency and that humans create their own actions. Foreknowledge seems to conflict with that freedom.
This argument is not fully developed here, but it points to the problem of divine foreknowledge.

The First Argument: Alif and Lam Indicate Generality
First argument using the grammatical rule of الاستغراق to prove all praise belongs to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the grammatical rule of the definite article (alif lam) is used as a theological proof that all praise belongs to Allah alone.

Core Idea
The definite article in 'al-hamdu' means all praise belongs to Allah, not creatures.

This first argument may seem like a simple grammatical point, but it carries profound theological weight. By analyzing the Arabic phrase 'Al-hamdu lillah', the author demonstrates that the definite article 'al-' indicates comprehensive generality (istighraq). This forces a crucial question: if any praise were genuinely due to creatures for their own actions, the phrase would be inaccurate. Thus, the argument leads to the Sunni doctrine that Allah creates all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise structure: a premise, an implication, and a conclusion.
The author uses grammatical reasoning as a foundation for a theological claim.
2
Identify the key grammatical term
Focus on the term 'istighraq' (generality) and how the definite article 'al-' functions in Arabic to indicate comprehensiveness.
3
Connect to the broader theological debate
Ask yourself: why does this grammatical point matter for the question of who creates actions? The commentary will explain how it supports the Sunni position.
This argument is the first of four in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن الألف واللام في قوله : ( الحمد لله ) يفيدان الاستغراق والاستغراق يوجب أن تكون كل المحامد والمدائح الله
First: Verily the alif and lam in His statement 'Al-hamdu lillah' indicate generality, and generality requires that all praises and commendations be for Allah.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is the first of four arguments proving that the Quranic phrase 'Al-hamdu lillah' (all praise is due to Allah) is only valid according to the Sunni position. The author argues that the definite article 'al-' in 'al-hamdu' indicates inclusiveness (istighraq), meaning that all types of praise belong to Allah in a comprehensive sense. This universality would be compromised if any praise were genuinely attributable to creatures for their own actions. Therefore, the servant cannot be the creator of his own good deeds; otherwise, he would deserve some of that praise. Thus, the phrase 'Al-hamdu lillah' is only correct if all praise ultimately returns to Allah, which is the Sunni doctrine that Allah creates all actions.

Key Terms
الألف واللام
الاستغراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The alif lam is just a grammatical point without theological significance.
Correction: In kalam, grammatical rules are used as proofs for doctrines. Here, the definite article's generality is leveraged to establish a foundational theological truth.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the definite article 'al-' in 'al-hamdu' according to the first argument?
A) It specifies a particular praise
B) It indicates generality and inclusiveness
C) It shows that some praise belongs to creatures
D) It is just for emphasis

Key Takeaway
The first argument uses the definite article in 'al-hamdu' to establish that all praise is due to Allah alone, implying Allah creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive praise from others, do you ever feel a sense of personal ownership over your good deeds? How does the truth that all praise belongs to Allah challenge that feeling right now?
?
Can you honestly say that every moment of gratitude you feel for your own abilities or achievements is ultimately directed back to Allah as the sole Creator of those actions? What would change if you lived that fully?
?
Think of a specific person you have praised or admired for their character or success. Based on this lesson, where does the credit truly belong? How can you realign your heart and speech to reflect 'Al-hamdu lillah'?

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Second Argument: Servant as Creator of Faith
Second argument against servant creating faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical consequence of attributing the creation of faith to the servant: it would invert the order of praise and contradict the Qur'anic declaration that all praise belongs to Allah.

Core Idea
If the servant creates faith, he deserves more praise than Allah.

This lesson presents the second argument from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah against the notion that the servant creates his own faith. The argument uses a comparison of actions: if faith is the most honorable creation, then its creator deserves the most praise. This would lead to a contradiction with the fundamental Islamic statement that all praise is for Allah. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the full argument before analysis.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument relies on the premise that faith and knowledge are the most honorable of created things.
This premise is explicitly stated in the passage.
3
Follow the logical chain
If the servant creates faith, his action is better than Allah's action, so he deserves more praise than Allah.
This leads to a contradiction with the phrase 'Al-hamdu lillah'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that the servant cannot be the creator of his own faith, because it would upset the order of praise.
This conclusion reinforces divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن العبد لو كان هو الموجد للإيمان والطاعات ، لكان فعله أحسن من فعل الله تعالى . لأن أشرف المخلوقات هو العلم والإيمان . ومن كان فعله أشرف من فعل غيره ، كان استحقاقه للحمد أعظم من استحقاق غيره فلو كان الايمان حصل بإيجاد العبد ، لوجب أن يكون استحقاق العبد للحمد ، أكمل من استحقاق الله له . وذلك يقدح في صحة قولنا : ( الحمد لله ) .
Second: If the servant were the originator of faith and acts of obedience, then his action would be better than the action of Allah the Exalted, because the most honorable of created things are knowledge and faith. And whoever's action is more honorable than the action of another, his deserving of praise is greater than the deserving of another. So if faith were obtained by the servant's origination, then necessarily the servant's deserving of praise would be more complete than Allah's deserving of it. And that impugns the correctness of our statement 'Al-hamdu lillah'.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is the second argument. It assumes that if the servant were the originator (mujid) of faith and obedience, then his action would be better than Allah's action because faith and knowledge are the most honorable created things. Since whoever creates something more honorable deserves more praise, the servant would deserve more praise than Allah, contradicting the verse that all praise is for Allah. Hence, the servant cannot create his own faith and obedience.

Key Terms
الموجد
الإيمان
الطاعات
الحمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant can create his own faith and acts of obedience.
Correction: This argument shows that if the servant created faith, he would deserve more praise than Allah, which contradicts 'Al-hamdu lillah'. Therefore, only Allah creates faith and obedience.

Checkpoint
If the servant created his own faith, what would be the consequence regarding praise?
A) The servant would deserve less praise than Allah
B) Allah would still deserve all praise
C) The servant would deserve more praise than Allah
D) The phrase 'Al-hamdu lillah' would become ambiguous

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that attributing the creation of faith to the servant leads to an inversion of praise, making the servant more praiseworthy than Allah, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute your faith and good deeds to your own efforts, does that thought diminish your gratitude to Allah? How does this argument dismantle that pride? Commit to saying 'Al-hamdu lillah' with full awareness after reading this.
?
Have you ever felt that your religious achievements (e.g., memorizing Qur'an, praying) are something you 'created'? How does the impossibility of the servant deserving more praise than Allah challenge that feeling? What will you do to remind yourself that faith is a gift?
?
If Allah alone is worthy of all praise for every good thing, including your own faith, then what attitude should you have toward your own shortcomings? Does this argument make you more reliant on Allah's mercy? Write one concrete action you will take today to express that reliance.
